MEXICO
LOW INCOME HOUSING PROJECT
LOAN AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:      Banco Nacional de Obras y Servicios Publicos, S.A. (BANOBRAS)
Guarantor:     United Mexican States
Executing      Fideicomiso Fondo Nacional de Habitaciones Populares (FONHAPO)
Agency:
Amount:        US$150.0 million equivalent.
Terms:         15 years, including 3 years of grace, at the
standard variable interest rate.
Relending      Loan proceeds would be provided to FONHAPO by the Government
Terms:         as equity contributions.  The proceeds would be relent by
FONHAPO to states, municipalities, social and cooperative
organizations and authorized banking institutions on terms and
conditions which vary with the type of sub-borrower and the
unit cost of sub-project facilities.
Project        (i) to increase the supply of public housing investment
Objectives:    provided to the poorest and largest segment of the housing
market; (ii) to strengthen FONHAPO during a critical
expansionary phase; and (iii) to support the Government in
addressing sector issues.
Project        The project would finance sub-projects for the construction of
Description:   serviced sites and progressive construction units in rapidly
growing medium size cities. FONHAPO would onlend funds to
sub-borrowers who would implement sub-projects, participate in
their financing, provide collateral guarantees and allocate
constructed facilities to eligible beneficiaries (non wage
workers earning less than 2.5 minimum wages) under appropriate
mortgage arrangements. In addition, the project would provide
technical assistance to FONHAPO required to support an
increased lending program and to FO-HAPO's sub-borrowers.
Finally, the project would support the carrying out of a
Housing Finance Study. The project would permit the
settlement of about 142,300 families in serviced lots of which
about 15,200 would include starter dwelling units.
Project Risks: The main project risks are: (1) the possible reduction in
FONHAPO's operational efficiency through its rapid growth; (2)
the possible shortage of land sites of adequate quality,
quantity and price; (3) possible discontinuity in the
Government's housing sector policy; (4) eventual shortfalls or
delays in beneficiaries repayments to sub-borrowers which
would raise the level of implicit subsidy in the project.

MEXICO
LOW-INCOME HOUSING PROJECT
Staff Appraisal Report
I. THE SECTOR
A. Main Features of Demand and Supply
1.01      Size and Characteristics of Housing Demand:  However measured,
and there are problems of measurement, Mexico's housing deficit is large.
Official estimates, drawn in year 1980, put it at 4.5 million units. The
national housing problem is compounded by: (a) a large demographic base
(76 million) with a sustained rate of growth (2.9% per annum); (b) a rapid
pace of urbanization (centers with more than 2,500 inhabitants grew at over
5% per year during the last decade, passing from 59% to 68Z of total
population); (c) the spatial polarization of a large share of housing
demand (26.5% of Mexicans live in three main metropolitan areas); (d) the
extreme dispersion of the rest (the country has 146 cities of intermediate
size and 95,000 settlements with less than 2,500 inhabitants); and (e) the
uneven financial capacity of the consumers (72% of the economically active
population earns incomes of less than 3 minimum wages, which in present
terms is the equivalent of US$425 per month. (Chart VI-1, p. 101).
1.02      Unregulated (Informal) Housing Supply:  Like  other developing
countries, Mexico has a segmented structure of housing supply. Most
additions to the stock are not of up-to-code -standard' units. During the
past decade, as much as 65% of new housing was supplied either by consumers
themselves or by informal builders operating at the margin of the building
codes, the established construction industry and the regulated financial
markets. At present, this percentage could be as low as 45%, due mostly to
the intensification of public housing programs and the impact of the
recession on private initiatives.
1.93      In the three main metropolitan areas (Mexico City, Guadalajara,
Monterrey) as in many of the fast growing medium size cities, most of the
new housing units are built on subdivisions opened up by clandestine and
often unscrupulous developers on illegally occupied sites (generally on
public property) with inadequate public services and, almost always, with
infringements to planning ordinances and building codes. The lack of legal
titles and inspection clearances prevents the buyers f-om using land and
improvements as acceptable collateral for new loans and forces them to
continue to operate on the unregulated credit market. And because it is
unregulated and operates with higher risk, loan conditions in this segment
of the market are disadvantageous to the borrowers.



- 2 -
1.04      Regulated Housing Supply:  The Government intervenes in the
housing market through a large number of institutions, each with its own
norms of operation and financial arrangements. Schematically, they can be
grouped in three main categories:
(a)   financial agents, mainly nationalized commercial banks, regulated by
the Bank of Mexico, which in addition to making mortgages at
commercial terms, apply a msndatory reserve on their total
liabilities (currently 8.8%) in form of *social interest" housing
loans; these constitute the so called Programa Financiero de Vivienda
or PFV, supported by a second-tier public agency (FOVI-FOGA) which
finances and insures such loans. Although somewhat different in
statute and controlled by the Secretariat of Urban Development and
Ecology (SEDUE), the Fondo de Habitaciones Populares (FONHAPO) can
also be included in this category, since it operates as a wholesale
lender and is empowered to borrow funds from FOVI-FOGA and from the
market;
(b)   the three payroll funds, financed through a 5% compulsory
contribution on all wages and salaries paid by the orivate sector
(INFONAVIT), the public bureaucracy (FOVISSSTE) and the military
(FOVIMI-ISSFAM); and
(c)   the non-financial public housing agencies, supported by Federal
or State treasury transfers, part of which come under the supervision
of SEDUE, such as the State Housing Institutes or the Regional
Development Funds, while others are autonomously managed as part of
larger sector programs, such as the housing schemes for rural workers
(PIDER), the employees of the petroleum industry (PEMEX) and the
electricity corporation (CFE). The Federal District (DF) also has a
housing scheme for its own employees.
1.05      Dimensions of Supply:  Government sources have estimated that
11 million units should be built by the year 2000, to add to and partly to
replace the 1980 housing stock of 12.2 million units. Although staggering,
these figures are not alarming wheTi seen in light of past achievements.
During the 1970's, housing output averaged about 393,000 units per year,
with additions to the stock exceeding the rate of population growth. In an
economy that grew at an extraordinarily fast pace, the construction
industry grew at an even faster pace, averaging 12% annually over the
expansionary cycle from 1978 to 1982. During the past sexenio (1977-1982)
the regulated housing sector, supported by concessionary credit,
contributed about 135,000 units per year reaching a peak of 263,100 units
in 1982. The corresponding investment, measured in 1980 prices, was of
USS10.7 billion. The payroll funds accounted for 63% of this investment
total, the public agencies for 9Z and the banking system financed the
remaining 28%. Only about one fourth of housing loans made by the banks
was onlent at commercial terms, to support the highly cyclical activities
of private contractors operating almost exclusively for the upper segment
of the market. Over the same period, unregulated housing production may
have been on the order of 250,000 units per year. Of interest is the
substantial reduction in the real cost of the average unit produced by the
public sector during this period (-17%), which was achieved through changes
in the structure of supply and the general adoption of less expensive
design standards. In current dollars equivalent, the cost of the average
unit dropped from USS8,950 in 1977 to US$7,350 in 1982.



- 3 -
1.06       Supply Distortions:  The recent expansion and favourable past
performance did not, in themselves, resolve Mexico's housing problem. They
may in fact have aggravated some of its aspects, mainly those related to
efficiency and equity in the allocation of resources. First, most of the
credit programs remained beyond the reach of the poorest and needy
population. Second, improper lending terms caused a very low average rate
of capital recovery (about 40% of real value over the period) and the
transfer of some 100 billion pesos (about US$4.0 billion) to the consumers
of public housing: a sum that at average 1980 costs would have financed 35%
more units than those actually built during the sexenio.l/ Thirdly, the
massive structure of the payroll funds, financed througihdistortionary
earmarked taxes, was characterized by inefficient and inequitable
allocation criteria.
1.07      The 1982 Crisis and Beyond:  1982 was a turning point in the
evolution of the Mexican construction industry. There was a sudden, severe
drop in output and over half of the 1.9 million workers employed by the
industry in 1981 were reportedly laid off. Though necessary and
unpostponable, the current stabilization plan has had an enormous social
cost. The GOM is sensitive to the limits to which the social system may be
strairned without unrest and disruption. In fact, it has implemented
several measures to attempt to ward-off critical ruptures. Moreover and
more importantly, the Government is aware of the need for speedv recovery
in employment levels, once the basic macroeconomic balance has been
restored. The present recession and the slower growth expected through the
rest of the decade, as compared to the 1970's, heightens the importance of
employment generation as a policy concern in the coming years. Because of
its characteristics, the construction industrv, and housing programs in
particular, will have a central role in policies aimed at economic recovery
with rapid employment growth.
B. The Medium Term Housing Plan 1983-1988
1.08      Main Features:  President de la Madrid's Administration has
introduced a Medium Term Housing Plan for the 1983-1988 period (the PNDV or
Programa Nacional para el Desarrollo de Vivienda) which:
(a)    establishes a new institutional framework for programming and
monitoring public housing investment with the new Ley Federal de
Vivienda (February 1984), which should make more effective theTInk
between public spending and housing policy;
(b)    allocates extraordinary resources to the sector with the 1984
Programa Estrategico de Vivienda (US$760 million in addition to the
USS1,940 million of the authorized budget);
(c)    reduces the role of the payroll funds, (down from 63% to 49% of
planned regulated investment);
(d)    expands the role of the financial agents (from 28% to 44%) and
improves substantially the rate of capital recovery in "social
interest housing credits by Introducing (in March 1984) new
financial rules for both the batnking system and the FONHAPO
programs;
(e)    stimulates private savings and -rivate construction of medium income
finished housing through the modified terms of FOVI-FOGA;
1/ The issues reviewed in this Chapter have been discussed at some length
in the report: Public Housing Policy in Mexico, Urban Sector Report
No. 4993-ME (Green Cover) p. 92, IBRD, June 1984.



- 4 -
(f)    keeps to a minimum the contribution of the non-financial public
agencies (down from 9% to 6% of expected regulated investment);
(g)    concentrates budgetary transfers on the cost-effective FONHAPO
program (increasing from 3% to 14% of regulated investment), which
is tailored for the poorer segments of the population and their
capacity to pay; and
(h)    favors cooperative community efforts organized at local level with
appropriate technologies and labor-intensive construction methods.
1.09      Proposed Role of Regulated Housing Agencies:  The Government's
strategy, as reflected in the Medium Term Housing Plan, aims to achieve the
desired targets of housing production by simply assigning different rates
of growth to different agents of supply. This course of action (probably
dictated by the political rigidities of the existing system) does not
address the need for structural change and still implies a questionable use
of resources, as will be discussed below. The changing role of different
housing agencies, with respect to their past and present activities, can be
gleaned from the following table:

1.10      Quantitative Aspects:  The Medium Term Housing Plan, echoes the
recommendations made by COVE at the end of 1982.2/ It represents a
rational response to then prevailing Mexican conditions, but its structure
and quantitative targets would now require further elaboration. The
revised 1984 volume of sector investment, complemented in March by a 40%
.strategic allocation, amounted to a substantial MexS357 billion (US$2.7
billion) which has probablv exceeded the target of 1% of GDP set for
previous annual programs and is expected to generate 270,000 units. By
1988, the original Plan expected the realization of 1.6 million units,
doubling the supplv of housing from regulated programs with respect to the
previous sexenio.
21 COVI, the Interministerial Commission on Housing, formed at the end of
the previous Administration, formulated the basic estimates of housing
needs and traced possible scenarios for public intervention.



1.11      The target of 1.6 million new units would be achieved through a
mere 11% increase in real investments, which implies a reduction of the
average unit cost of production of 45% in real terms. Considering the
dramatic drop in unit prices already achieved during the last sexenio, this
calls for a dramatic expansion in the proportional output of least cost
housing solutions (sites and services and progressive construction units),
which are mainly financed by FONHAPO. The annual production of this agency
should therefore increase from 69,000 starts in 1984 to an improbable
200,000 starts in 1988, virtually triplicating its output over five years.
And since FONHAPO's growth largely depends on Government transfers, the
Plan is (at least implicitly) based on a substantial provision of budgetary
resources to the housing sector. This is to be made without the sacrifice
of the earmarked taxes contributed to the payroll funds or the compulsory
reserve requirements applied by the banks in -social interest mortgage
loans. Whether or not the fiscal budget will afford these initiatives, in
a new era of greater austerity in public spending i4 a matter that deserves
closer scrutiny.
C. Main Issues of the Sector
1.12      Public vs. Private Sector Role:  One issue not addressed by the
PLan is the extent to which public sector intervention is justified for the
supply of a private good such as housing.3/ Increasing public sector
expenditures for this purpose has been advocated ambivalently in terms of
anticyclical objectives (to exploit the large output multipliers and low
import coefficients of a construction industry with high rates of
unutilized capacity) and also in terms of distributional equity, pointing
to the free market's failure to provide affordable homes for the poor
and to the fact that appropriate shelter is recognized by the Mexican
Constitution as a basic right of all citizens. The 1984 Strategic Housing
Program concentrates budgetary contributions on minimum cost schemes,
particularly in the provision of land, infrastructure and progressive
construction assistance, for which the public sector's comparative
advantages over the private sector can be more readily justified. This
approach may help rationalize the construction initiatives of the informal
market and support local organizational efforts and employment generation,
but will have a rather muffled effect on the main construction industry.
It also clashes with the existing structure of supply which remains
dominated by agencies specialized in finished, up-to-code, "standardized"
housing, and still accounted for most of the sector's investment in 1984,
even after the drastic corrections made with the "strategic allocations.
The Fragmentation of Government Initiatives
1.13      Mexican authorities define as the "public sector's  housing
program the combination of investment activities by various types of
regulated institutions. However, there is no central financial authority
to oversee aU these institutions. Neither are there consolidated accounts
3/ According to a favorite indicator of Mexican housing authorities, the
public share of investment in housing increased from 0.9% of GDP in
1978 to 1.2% of GDP in 1982. This order of magnitude is not uncommon
in Latin American countries.  In Mexico puL ic sponsored housing
programs have been responsible for about 5% of gross capital formation
and up to 12.5% of public capital formation during the last sexenio.



- 6 -
for the system as a whole4/. Excluding the banks, the agencies involved
operate outside the mainstream of the financial system and therefore are
not directly regulated by the monetary authorities. Their funds are
alternatively provided (in the case of the public housing agencies) or
collected (in the case of the payroll funds) by the Secretariat of Finance
(SHCP), but their rules of operation and procedures for onlending are
determined by internal, agency specific arrangements. The resulting
proliferation of activities and the variety of credit terms and conditions
make it virtually impossible for the Government to carry out effective
accounting and budgeting of the aggregate sector investment and of the
implicit subsidy.
1.14      Low Rates of Capital Recovery:  With an average cost of funds for
the banking system on the order of 50% per annum and about the same level
of inflation in housing production prices, the generally low interest
rates on current mortgages involve considerable decapitalization, in spite
of the recently introduced instruments of progressive adjustment which are
described below.
1.15      In the FOV'-FOGA scheme (that of the banking system), the
starting interest rates are quite negative, varying between 15% and 30% and
are implicitly adjusted through the annual increment of loan repayments
(set at 70% of the minimum wage increases) until they level off with the
average cost of funds (CPP),5/ when amortization of principal would
actually begin. In substance, mortgage payments in the initial years are
wholly used to offset the accumulation of negative interest, until a
second-tier mortgage is made. Strictly speaking the subsidy applies only
for re-financing interest accrued on the loan, but it should be noted that
the banks are obliged to refinance all loans up to 70% of their original
value and that the resources used in these operations are charged against
the compulsory reserve requirements in the rubric of social interest
housing. In fact, this has led to a change in the volume and structure of
the bank's mandatory reserves. The Whole procedure is complex and
obfuscates the true measure of the subsidy in the effective rate of
interest, while trying to achieve two contradictory objectives: the
reduction of the overall subsidy and, at the same time, the containment of
debt service charges. Inevitably, the re-financing formula produces an
average rate of interest well below the average cost of funds (CPP) even in
the event that the starting rate would equal the CPP. Moreover, the
subsidized average rate is not known a priori.  In fact, the rate of
interest is a dependent variable and not a parameter of the system.
4/ Some basic data for the system as a whole have been compiled by COVI up
to 1980 (published in 1982) and since then in special tabulations
prepared by COVI or by the DGCPV-SEDUE. These agencies, though not
empowered to control housing credit institutions act as coordinators of
the public sector's effort.
5/ The average cost of funds (Costo Porcentual Promedio de Captacion) or
CPP is an index published each month by the Bank of Mexico, reflecting
the weighted average interest rate paid by the financial institutions
on time deposits in local currency.



1.16      In the FONHAPO scheme, which must be affordable to a much poorer
target group, the payments are fully kept in line with the annual minimum
wage adjustments, but the starting interest rates are set as low as 9X up
to a maximum 15X p.a., and are upgraded each year during the life of a loan
according to a factor of 1.1. Effect.ve rates are somewhat higher than
nominal rates because they are charged monthly, while amortization of
principal is applied annually (para. 5.10).
1.17      The payroll funds instead continue to offer mortgages at a flat
rate of 4% per annum! Should inflation persist at the present high annual
rates the level of capital recovery might range between 25% and 50% for the
payroll funds, would hover about 60% for FONHAPO and possibly reach 70% for
the PFV. In Mexico, as in the Bank, it is generally assumed that inflation
will follow a descending path over the years to come. Since all Mexican
schemes of housing credit are linked to the minimum wage and the real value
of the latter is likely to be gradually readjusted, the relative rates of
capital recovery will probably improve from the present low ebb.
Nevertheless, all current credit schemes involve a sizeable amount of
implicit subsidy and some of them are barely replicable.6/  Wbat is more
disturbing is that the financial rules now in place makeit difficult to
monltor, lest to predict, the actual amount of public subsidy involved as
well as the long-term sustainability of housing programs.
1.18      The Distribution of Housing Subsidies:  The question is not
whether "social interest" housing should or should not bear subsidies but
rather to determine a level for such subsidies which may be tolerable for
the Mexican economy and to ensure their appropriate distribution to
"deserving" social groups. Under the present system, the bulk of housing
subsidies goes to the beneficiaries of the payroll funds and to those of
the regulated banking system, many of which could afford higher interest
mortgages. A rough estimate of the total public subsidy involved in the
1984 investment program is on the order of US$1.4 billion, of which nearly
half (47%) attributable to the payroll funds, over one third (35%) to the
FOVI-FOGA system and some 14% to the public agencies. FONHAPO accounted
for only 4% of the total housing subsidy while contributing 26% of the new
housing units.
1.19      Although the payroll funds are limited to workers earning less
than five minimum wages and should favor the lower wage workers, their
impact is probably regressive. These programs are incapable of attending
but a small portion of their captive demand. Yet, aside from making it
difficult for account holders to withdraw deposits made in their behalf,
the funds fail to pay either interest or monetary correction on the
individual accounts. Moreover, the payroll tax is probably shifted on to
prices and may have a regressive incidence on the distribution of family
budgets. And, because it leads to an increase in unit labor costs, the tax
may also have an indirect unemployment effect.
6/ WUD advisory staff considers a housing project replicable when the
investor (in this case the Government) recovers sufficient cash flow to
finance a repeater project of the same size. To the extent that such
decision implies a stable commitment of public resources, social
replicability could be achieved with a cost recovery (in real terms) as
low as 50%. However, any increase in the volume of the repeater
investment entails incremental public transfers unless returns on the
prior investment are greater than 50%.



- 8 -
1.20      The 1984 structure of bank mortgages under the PFV will
concentrate credits on families with incomes in excess of four minimum
wages (i.e. belonging to the top two deciles of the income distribution:
see Chart VI-1). Given the fact that the PFV is implemented through the
initiatives of private developers (who subsequently transfer bank loans to
the final buyers) it is likely that, as in the past, the actual patterm of
investment be heavily tilted to the upper limit of the PFV allowable income
brackets, presently set at 7.5 minimum wages.
1.21      This leaves to the informal housing market the immense task of
attending to the shelter needs of some 70% of the Mexican population. Only
a minor portion of the total fiscal resources allocated in various forms of
concessionary housing credit is assigned to FONHAPO to assist this market.
In this respect, should the FONHAPO program be successful in rapidly
expanding the supply of appropriate shelter solutions with a much lower
fiscal subsidy per household than the conventional public housing programs,
a consensus may gradually develop in the direction of supporting its
further growth by diverting to it funds from other sources.
D. Bank Strategy
1.22      The Government has officially stated its objective of improving
the public management of subsidies. Specifically, this involves
controlling and budgeting the subsidy element of preferential credit and
making it explicit and transparent. For credit components under
Bank-financed projects in sectors other than housing, the Government's
commitment to the control and gradual reduction of subsidies was formalized
in the General Agreement on Interest Rates (GIRA). Signed in August 1984,
and effective since November 1984, the GIRA framework focusses on the
progressive adjustment of preferential interest rates in accordance with
variations to a central reference rate, the CPP (see footnote 5 on page 6).
1.23      Housing credit was not included in the GIRA because of the
conceptual difference in the adjustment mechanism applied to such credits.
The Government felt that adjustment through linkages with the minimum wage
was socially and politically more acceptable in the case of housing credit,
since this formula offers a more direct expression of affordability and can
be easily explained to and understood by the target population. However,
the current onlending terms for housing credit present obvious difficulties
in respect to the Government's objective of improving the public management
of subsidies. Because of their sensitivity to inflation trends and minimum
wage policies, these credit terms result in subsidy transfers which remain
unpredictable, uncontrollable and not transparent.
1.24      The proposed project would be the first in a series of lending
operations in the housing sector and would support the establishment of a
sector policy dialogue. The project would address the issue of capital
recovery and measurement of subsidies for the FONHAPO program. While
FONHAPO is presently responsible for only 5% of the regulated housing
investment, the Government has targetted a rapid increase in the relative
share of funds devoted to this program by 1988. The proposed loan would
therefore assist the Government in pursuing a stated objective of fiscal
policy during the critical expansionary phase of FONHAPO, while achieving
greater distributional equity in the sectoral allocation of resources.



-9-
1.25      At the same time, the project would support the carrying out of a
housing finance study which would address system-wide issues such as
replicability, cost recovery, measurement of subsidies, the adequacy of the
payroll retention system, and the impact of general housing policy on
financial savings, employment and the budget. The Government would forward
terms of reference to the Bank, for comment, by December 31, 1985 (a first
draft is proposed in Annex VIII), and exchange views with the Bank on the
progress of the study by March 31, 1986. The Study would be finalized by
December 31, 1986, at which time the Government would discuss results and
conclusions with the Bank. Future housing loans of the Bank would support
gradual reform of sector wide issues as addressed and discussed in
connection with the study.
Rationale for Bank Involvement.
1.26      By supporting the FONHAPO program, the Bank would be endorsing
the Government's objectives of re-directing an increasing percentage of
total public housing investment to the poorest and largest segment of the
housing market. The rationale for the proposed loan would be to strengthen
capital recovery and to develop better criteria for future replicability of
the program after the critical mintial phase of FONHAPO's expansion. Bank
participation would complement necessary initial budgetary allocations,
ensure maintenance of GOM commitment to this window of housing credit and
require FONHAPO to meet specific standards of financial performance,
thereby gradually increasing the agency's own contribution towards new
investment financing, and reducing the need for incremental fiscal
transfers. The proposed project would provide technical assistance
required by the projected growth of FONHAPO's investment program. Finally,
the project would be beneficial towards the establishment and maintenance
of a comprehensive housing policy dialogue with the Government.
E. Bank Experience with Past Lending in the Sector
1.27      Past Loans:  Bank involvement in Mexican housing and urban
development activities began seven years ago, with Loan 1554-ME signed in
April 1978 (US$16.5 million) which has been fully disbursed. This project,
located in the Lazaro Cardenas Conurbation, constituted a satisfactory
experiment with the provision of serviced lots and progressive construction
units to about 1000 low income families. A second project was signed in
December 1981 (Loan 1990-NE of US$164 million) comprising a sites and
services component for 1,900 units, distributed in 10 cities of the Gulf
Region. The new Government has confirmed interest in the original
objectives of this loan, but has requested some of its administrative
arrangements to be revised in light of the new institutional organization
for regional development. A third project is currently in course of
implementation (Loan 2194-ME of US$9.2 million signed in August 1982),
supporting the preparation of the pre-investment stage of a development
program for the Mexico City-Region, which will also involve land and
housing development schemes.



- 10 -
1.28      The Bank has been assisting Mexico also in the implementation of
a number of urban infrastructure projects. In June 1973, a US$90 million
loan was signed to support bulk water supply for the Mexico City
metropolitan area (Loan 909-ME now closed). Three other loans for water
supply and sewerage projects were channeled to local companies through
FIFAPA, a specialized Government Trust Fund (Loan 1186-ME of US$40 million,
signed in January 1976, Loan 1913-ME of US$125 million, signed in November
1980, and Loan 2281-ME of US$100.3 million, signed in September 1983).
Mexican performance under these loans is satisfactory in terms of sub-loan
commitment and execution. Compliance with tariff increases required by
loan covenants can be considered adequate in the majority of cities, given
the critical economic situation of the country. Urban infrastructure
components have been satisfactorily implemented with Bank participation
also for projects in the tourism sector (Loans 793-ME of US$22 million,
December 1971; 1420-ME of US$42 million, May 1977; and 1524-ME of US$50
million, March 1978).
F. Sector Work and EDI Seminar
1.29      Two sector reports in the Housing and Urban Development sector
(IBRD No. 4939-ME and IBRD No. 4993a-ME) have been produced in Green Cover
in March and June, 1984 respectively, and informally discussed with
Government officials. Two other urban sector reports concerning
Territorial Development and Fiscal Devolution are presently in Yellow Cover
stage (IBRD-5188a-ME of June 1984).  A Sector Report on Water and Sewerage
Policy has been completed in April 1984 (IBRD Nb. 4840a-ME).
1.30      At the invitation of the GOM, EDI has helped organize a Seminar
on Urban Management which took place in Mexico City during October 1980,
following the publication of the first Mexican Housing Plan. Bank
experiences with low-income housing projects in several Latin American
countries were reviewed by operational staff with the authors of the
Housing Plan and with senior officials from several States of Mexico.
II.  THE BORROWER, THE TRUST FUND, THE SUB-BORROWERS AND THE
FINAL BENEFICIARIES
The Borrower (BANOBRAS)
2.01      The loan would be made to the National Bank for Public Works and
Services (BANOBRAS), which is a financial agent of the Government, legally
empowered to act as a World Bank Borrower. BANOBRAS is a public
development bank, which obtains its funds from the Mexican Government and
the international market. According to BANOBRAS Organization Law, the
Federal Government controls its policies and supervises its lending and
borrowing activities. The Secretariat of Finance and Public Credit (SHCP)
has veto power over the resolutions of BANOBRAS' Board of Directors and
approves BANOBRAS yearly budgets.  BANOBRAf is authorized to carry out such
other functions related to its credit activities as may be approved by
SHCP.



- 11 -
2.02      BANOBRAS operates several trust funds, each corresponding to a
specific investment program, such as FIFAPA (the water and sewerage fund
partially supported by three Bank loans); the FM or Municipal Development
Fund (supported by an IDB loan) which finances a variety of urban
facilities; FONHAPO (the low-income housing fund), and others. In
addition, BANOBRAS makes direct loans to States, Municipalities and other
public sector enterprises and is functioning as the financial intermediary
for a number of World Bank loans in urban development, highways and
railways.
The Trust Fund (FONHAPO)
2.03      Brief History:  From 1949 to December 1980, the Fideicomiso Fondo
Nacional de Habitaciones Populares (National Trust Fund for Low-Income
Housing, known by the acronym of FONHAPO) existed merely as a division of
BANOBRAS, which had the mandate to construct low-cost housing. Two changes
in the legislation under which BANOBRAS operates (December 31, 1980 and
1981), granted FONHAPO its own administration as a trust fund of the
Government ("fideicomiso") and a new mandate to finance the construction of
non-conventional housing types for low-income households as envisaged in
the National Housing Program. The programs in which the World Bank would
participate began in 1982.
2.04      The formal establishment of FONHAPO as a public credit agency
took place through a Decree published in the Diario Oficial de la Republica
on April 2, 1981 and the Agreement of May 13, 1981, stipulated between the
Goverament through the Secretariat of Finance and Public Credit (SHCP) as
the sole Trustor (the fideicomitente or the entity who owns the fund) and
BANOBRAS, as the Trustee (the fiduciaria or the entity which administers
the fund). This Agreement indicated that the then Secretariat of Human
Settlements and Public Works (SAHOP, which is now SEDUE or the Secretariat
of Urban Development and Ecology) would acquire tutorial and normative
responsibility for the Trust Fund, and that funds would be allocated for
FONHAPO's operations as part of the yearly budget of this Secretariat.7/
2.15      Organization of FONHAPO:  the Fund has a Board of Directors
(Comite Tecnico y de Tjistribuclon de Fondos) which is chaired by the
Secretary of SEDUE and includes representatives of otber concerned
Secretariats (SHCP and SPP), of the banking sector (BANOBRAS and FOVI -
Banco de Mexico) as well as the Federal Controller, a Secretary and
FONHAPO's General Director. Internally, seven Departments (Gerencias)
report to the General Director. These include Administration, Finance,
Real Estate, Legal, Technical, Planning, and Social Promotion. In
addition, there are the Operational Coordination office, and that of the
Internal Controller (Chart II-1 in Annex). FONHAPO's headquarters employ a
qualified and motivated staff of about 300, more than half of which at
7/ The idea of establishing FONHAPO dates back to 1979, when an
intersecretarial committee (COVI) was formed under the coordination of
SAHOP, to carry out the technical, financial, administrative and
economic studies necessary to frame the objectives of the new Fund in a
general context of public housing policy. This preparatory work was
concluded in December 1980, when the Secretariat of Planning and
Budgeting (SPP) recommended to the Presidency to alter the structure
and activities of a fund operated by BANOBRAS.



- 12 -
professional level. In addition, nine regional offices have been recently
created to assist locally sub-borrowers in the preparation and
implementation of projects and to supervise their execution. FONHAPO also
keeps a roster of pre-qualified consultants to provide professional
assistance to sub-borrowers under contract.
2.06      A detailed description of the Statutory Objectives, Sources of
Financing, Rules of Operation, and of the current Terms and Conditions for
onlending of FONHAPO is presented in Annex II. The main features of the
typical sub-loans are summarized in Table 4 (para. 5.08). Typical
amortization tables are supplied in Tables 5.4 and 5.5 and in Charts V-1
and V-2.
2.07      Performance to Date:  Since the inception of the new Government
in January 1982, FONHAPO has rapidly expanded its operations to the 31
States of Mexico with a total of over 250 sub-loans to date. During 1984,
it has invested the equivalent of US$107 million (June 30, exchange rate)
of which 25% in Sites and Services, 59% in Progressive Construction
Dwellings, 16% in Home Upgrading. In terms of umits financed, 36,000 were
completed at the end of 1984, and about 69,300 have been started, to be
completed in 1985. Sub-loan preparations for 1985, include 98,000 new
units.
2.08      FONHAPO's records have been accurately kept, separating the new
from the old portfolio, and an examination of its accounting and auditing
procedu-es has proven satisfactory. The Fund maintains its own monitoring
and evaluation program and bases its internal planning and financial
projections on the marketing and implementation results achieved in the
prior years. The disbursement period on a sample of 105 implemented
sub-projects examined by the mission averaged less than one year. Under
the new terms of onlending, sub-loans reach maturity in four to seven
years, which woul_ allow FONHAPO to reinvest two to three times the funds
received during the period of Bank loan repayment.
2.09      Growth Management:  FONHAPO is already, and will be be more so in
the future, a prime lender for housing projects meeting its requirements.
During the three years it has been operating under the new statute the
agency has competently managed its own growth. The present management's
awareness of the problems of rapid expansion is revealed by the attention
paid to development of a new organizational structure, with appropriate
internal controls and a suitable information system. The Comite Tecnico
(Loan Committee) which is responsible for the evaluation of all loans,
receives a complete information package, much of it computerized, for its
monthly meetings. In addition to the timely prepared monthly financial
statements, the report contains recent information of all loans granted and
in process under the new programs. To control loan processing and
operations, operation manuals have been recently revised. A good example
of these is the new procedural guide for the regional offices
(Instrumentacion de las Delegaciones Regionales).  Another example is the
borrower's guide on loan processing (Instructivo para la Elaboracion de los
Formatos para la Contratacion del Cretito). Finally, the whole structure
of FONHAPO is being reorganized on the basis of an Organization Manual
(Manual de Organizacion) prepared during 1984 by a team of management
consultants, which details the new structure of the Fund and specifies the
functions and responsibilities of its various branches (Charts IV-1 to IV-9
in Annex IV).



- 13 -
2.10      Possible Improvements:  further progress can be achieved in three
main directions: (i) advances in computerization; FONHAPO has undertaken
a modest program of mechanization, which has been slowed down by budget
constraints imposed by the Mexican Government: completion of this
computerization program would be supported by the Bank under the Technical
Assistance Component; (ii) better financial information: the Finance
Department should be encouraged to produce more details in its monthly
financial statements on interest income by program, interest charged on
overdue accounts and a more complete analysis of the new portfolio by
dividing it into interest and non-interest bearing portions; (iii)
borrower's guidance on collection: PONHAPO should consider designing a
standard collection system for its borrowers, with manuals for organizing
field collection offices, mechanization of collection records, and enforce
its use by FONHAPO's borrowers (para. 4.13).
The Sub-borrowers
2.11      FONHAPO channels its credits to sub-borrowers, which are
responsible for the implementation of the sub-projects and the allocation
of the facilities built to a pre-identified group of beneficiaries.
sub-borrowers are authorized to apply a one point spread above the
applicable FONHAPO interest rate an the mortgage contracts they stipulate
with final beneficiaries, which is considered adequate to cover the
sub-borrowers' administrative costs in view of the supplemental assistance
furnished by FONHAPO. FONHAPO controls the process of allocation (para.
4.12), but has no direct contractual relationship with the ultimate
beneficiaries of its lines of credit. The intermediary agents of FONIHAPO
can be:
i)    Federal public sector institutions, when their schemes and
budgets for low-income housing developments have been approved by
the Federal Government;
ii)    State and Municipal governments, decentralized institutions and
parastatal or paramunicipal enterprises (most frequently State
Housing Instltutes), when developing low-income housing schemes
consistent with the objectives of FONHAPO;
iii)    authorized banking institutions operating in accordance with the
General Law of Credit Institutions and Auxiliary Organizations;
iv)    cooperative housing associations; and
v)    legally constituted groups, which are implementing programs
consistent with the norms of FONEAPO.
The Final Beneficiaries
2.12      FONHAPO's loans are destined to benefit a segment of Mexican
population which must be: (a) preferably choosen among non-wage workers;
(b) with heads of household receiving incomes no larger than 2.5 times the
minimum wage (about US$355 per month in January 1985);8/ (c) intending to
8/  Reference to the level of income of the head of household which is more
easily quantifiable than that of the household is geuierally adopted for
normative purposes in Mexico. As discussed in Annex VI (para. 5) total
family income per capita would represent a better measurement of the
affordability of debt service. However FONHAPO does collect data of
household income and household composition in its monitoring surveys of
the target group.



- 14 -
purchase a home in order to live in it; and (d) not owners of other real
estate property. In order to qualify for a credit from FONHAPO,
sub-borrowers are required to submit an analysis of potential beneficiaries
and FONHAPO's Loan Committee may reject selectively or totally the
corresponding documentation if insufficient evidence is submitted of: i)
the authenticity of the stated income of beneficiaries; ii) the absence of
other real estate property; or iii) the composition of the households
(para. 4.12).
2.13      From the 1984 Monitoring and Evaluation Survey of FONHAPO, one
learns that less than half (48.5%) of the beneficiaries of completed
projects, held a permanent job and the remainder were supporting themselves
with informal sector activities. Prior to access to the sub-projects, some
58% of the surveyed population used to spend 21% of their income to pay
rent, while the monthly payments on their FONHAPO-financed premises
averaged only 17% of their current income. The remaining 42% of residents
faced no special hardship in meeting the required monthly payments. The
average household size was of 5.1 people. In general, the survey
population had been previously residing in an urban area for approximately
10 years.
2.14      The analysis of a smaller, but carefully planned and executed
sample of families in sites and services projects, conducted by FONHAPO's
Monitoring and Evaluation Unit between May and July 1984 (details in
Annex VI), affords the following conclusions:
a)    FONHAPO's Sites and Services projects do reach their intended
target population, which constitutes a low, but not the lowest
segment of the income distribution (the mean income of the
household head is 1.33 MW (about US$189 per month) well below
the established level for FONHAPO (2.5 MW); the average family
income is expectedly higher, but still below 2.0 MW);
b)    this population is not reached, but exceptionally, by the other
existing Government housing programs;
c)    FONHAPO's product and the associated financial schemes are
affordable by the average family in the target population, even
when due allowance is granted for supplementary expenditures in
house-building.
2.15      These data confirm the effectiveness of the FONHAPO program.  An
explanation of its marketing success, particularly in medium size cities,
can be gleaned from Chart VI-1 in Annex, which illustrates the income
distribution of Mexican households. While the graphic represents the
findings of a national survey conducted in 1977, it must be noted that
income distribution in Mexico has shown a remarkably constant structure
over time and has probably not improved significantly since then, in light
of the recent recession of the economy. The 1980 Census analysis confirms
that the bulk of housing demand comes from households below the seventh
decile of income distribution (2.5 minimum wages).



- 15 -
III. THE PROJECT
Background
3.01      During the Annual Meetings of September 1983, the Mexican
Government expressed interest in a significant amount of World Bank support
to the housing sector. This first loan through FONHAPO has been prepared
for Bank approval in response to that request. Project preparation has
been based on the findings of a study on Public Housing Policy in Mexico
(IBRD-4993a-ME) which has been produced by Bank staff in June 1984.
3.02      The report on public housing policy in Mexico explains the
present structure of housing institutions and their very recent operational
reorganization, following the new federal housing law (Ley Federal de
Vivienda of December 29, 1983). Staff reviews of the report concluded that
early but limited circulation of its findings to Mexican authorities would
already serve as the foundation for a more intensive review, centered on
the financial issues encountered in the housing sector.
3.03      During discussions of the housing policy report and the
preparation of the proposed project, preliminary agreement was reached with
the Government to undertake a more analytical study on housing finance
through the joint efforts of the Bank and the four key Mexican agencies:
SHCP (finance), SPP (budget), SEDUE (housing policy), and Banco de Mexico.
The Government gave assurances during negotiations that it would carry out
a Housing Policy Study, and discuss the study's conclusions and
recommendations no later than December 31, 1986.
3.04      It is recognized that the Bank desire to support a gradual reform
of the Mexican housing finance system cannot be properly addressed on a
project specific basis, but only through a system-wide policy dialogue to
be developed over time, eventually supported by a sequence of lending
operations during the next five years. The proposed project should
therefore be interpreted as a first step towards concrete cooperation.
Project Objectives
3.05      The proposed project is aimed at the following objectives:
a) support replicability and growth of the FONHAPO program, which
focusses housing investment to the poorest and largest segment of the
housing market;
b) support FONHAPO during a critical expansionary phase by
promoting the application of new onlending terms while assuring specific
standards of financial performance and providing technical assistance for
institutional strengthening; and
c) support the Government in addressing sector issues.
Project Description
3.06      The loan would finance a slice (para. 3.09) of the 1985-1988
credit program of FONHAPO, a trust fund administered by BANOBRAS, which
would be the Borrower.   Counterpart funds would be appropriated annually



- 16 -
in the Government budget and passed on to FONHAPO as equity contributions.
FONHAPO would enter into Sub-loan Agreements, secured by collateral
guarantees, with States, Municipalities and social or cooperative
organizations for the preparation of serviced sites for low-income housing
schemes. FONHAPO will provide technical assistance to: (a) support
sub-borrowers in the preparation, appraisal, implementation, selection of
beneficiaries, allocation and transfer of facilities, collection of
charges, monitoring and evaluation of sub-projects; (b) establishment of a
computer program system for financial information; (c) designing and
promoting the use of collection models for its sub-borrowers; and (d)
carrying out a study to develop a medium term program.   Bank funds (the
proposed loan amount is US$150 million) would be used to partially finance
basic infrastructure works (earthworks, drainage, roads, sewerage, water
and electricity networks) and starter dwellings on the serviced lots in
which about 140,000 low-income households could settle. The target group
would be formed by households with incomes below 2.5 times the minimum
wage9/. They would be expected to make an initial deposit and to make
mortgage payments to the sub-borrowers indexed to the current value of
minimum wages. In parallel with the execution of the project, the
Government would carry out a joint study with the Bank on the national
housing finance system.
Project Costs
3.07      The estimated costs of the proposed project, excluding taxes, are
shown in the Summary Table (page vi). The appraisal of project cost has
been based on actual cost experience, corresponding to the average unit
prices of a sample of 105 sub-projects financed by FONHAPO during the first
half of 1984. These costs were expressed in constant 1984 dollar
equivalents (i.e. US$890 for a serviced lot of 117 m2 and US$3,700 for a
3-room unit of 38 m2). A flat rate of 10Z has been included for possible
physical increases in all components. The price escalation on the annual
investments has been calculated according to a seven year disbursement
profile (Bank-wide average for lines of credit) and to standard projections
of dollar inflation, in the assumption that corresponding adjustments in
the value of the local currency will maintain the exchange rate parity.10/
Bank Participation
3.08      The Bank would finance 50% of the total cost of selected
sub-projects, corresponding to the foreign exchange component (estimated at
25% of total costs) and an equivalent contribution towards local costs,
given the social nature of the project. Thus the loan would assist in
financing works equivalent to US$233 million and another US$67 million of
related expenditures in land purchases and preparations, professional,
9/ According to the 1980 Census, 72% of Mexico's economically active
population earns incomes below 3 minimum wages and 57% earns less than
1.6 minimum wages. Eligibility for FONHAPO's sub-loans is limited to
heads of households whose monetary income is below 2.5 minimum wages
(currently equivalent to US$355 per month). The figure of 2.0 MW is
usually interpreted as the absolute poverty level in Mexico.
10/ International inflation assumptions were made as follows: 1985 (5.0%);
1986 (7.5%); 1987-90 (8.0%); 1991 and thereafter (5.0%).



- 17 -
administrative and technical assistance services for a total of USS300
million equivalent. At current average costs this investment would permit
the settlement of 142,000 families in serviced lots and the financing of
15,200 starter dwelling units (Table 5.23 in Annex V).
3.09      The Bank-financed Project would represent about 19X of FONHAPO's
planned production of about 400,000 units under the 1985-1988 Medium Term
Program and about 11% of the 800,000 units expected under the 1985-1992
Long Term Program (both figures based on 1984 constant dollar prices).
Bank participation would be slanted in favor of Sites and Services rather
than Progressive Construction sub-projects (45% versus only 5% respectively
of the total units financed under these two lines of credit). In dollar
terms, about 2/3 of Bank disbursements would be made against sub-projects
of the first kind and the remaining 1/3 against progressive construction
sub-projects (compare Tables 5.12 and 5 22 in Annex V).
Criteria for Sub-project Selection
3.10      Selection of sub-projects for Bank financing would be based on
considerations of unit costs and affordability by the chosen target group
according to FONHAPO's general credit conditions (Table 4 on page 28) and
would have to meet the following additional criteria:
a) only sites and services and progressive construction sub-projects,
(i.e. financed under FONHAPOs two iain lines of credit, which account
for 85% of the agency's activities) (para. 7.03);
b) sub-projects above a certain size (e.g. costing not less than
US$500,000 equivalent) so as to reduce the Bank's supervision, and
disbursement activities to a manageable number of operations;
c)  sub-projects yielding a rate of financial return of not less than 9X
per annum; and
d) sub-projects located in the priority cities which the National Urban
Development Plan has singled out for preferential attention on the
basis of: (i) relative size and growth rate; (ii) specific role in the
national urban and productive systems; (iii) industrial development
potential; (iv) local entrepreneurial and management capacity; (v)
accessibility and area of Influence; (vi) diversity and quality of
available urban services; (vii) specific administrative role.ll/
Commitment of Bank Funds
3.11      Presentation to the Bank of Programs for commitment of Bank Loan
proceeds would be required annually by December 31 for each forthcoming
fiscal year and would be jointly reviewed in light of FONHAPO's annual
investment budgets. Sub-loan Agreements bearing Bank financing would be
sent to the Bank for approval.   Assuming that the average sub-project
11/ Bank loan proceeds would be directed to the 59 'strategic' medium-size
cities and to comunities on the fringes of the three metropolitan
areas (Mexico City, Guadalajara and Monterrey), complementing
investments supported by the Bank's FIFAPA credits for water and
sewerage networks and the IDB funded FM loans vhich are also directed
to these cities.



- 18 -
approved for Bank financing would cost US$1.5 to US$2.5 million equivalent
(in-keeping with the mean cost of FONHAPO's larger operations which include
750 to 1200 units) the Bank loan would support the financing of about
twenty five sub-projects per year.12!   Commitment of Loan Funds is
expected to be completed by December 31, 1990.
3.12      FONHAPO's processing of sub-loan applications follows a
structured three-step procedure (promotion, preparation and evaluation)
which is described in Chapter IV and involves an average period of 6 to 12
months. The Fund would continue to offer technical assistance to
sub-borrowers as needed during the three stages through its regional
offices. The central office would be mainly concerned with the general
administration of the program and the final steps of sub-loan approval.
Based on a review of the files of sample sub-projects in course of
execution Bank staff have found the regulations, forms and procedures
currently in use of acceptable quality.
Procurement
3.13       Mexicos's construction Industry is highly developed and capable
of executing works at costs competitive with international bidders and none
of the individual contracts would be large enough to attract foreign
firms. Therefore, most construction contracts would be awarded through
local competitive bidding procedures (LCB) acceptable to the Bank. The
sub-borrowers (States, Municipalities, building cooperatives and community
organizations) would be responsible for inviting bids, evaluating them and
awarding construction contracts under FONHAPO's supervision.13/ Mexican
Federal and State laws controlling procurement in publicly funded programs
provide a satisfactory competitive climate but differ from Bank policy and
guidelines on certain specific aspects.14/ Agreement was reached with
BANOBRAS to follow procurement procedures consistent with Bank guidelines
on those sub-projects which would be partially financed with loan proceeds.
12/ During the first seven months of 1984, FONHAPO has funded 105 sites and
services and progressive construction sub-loans. During 1983, 79
sub-loans were made in the two selected lines of credit.
13/ FONHAPO emphasizes the rapid and efficient execution of sub-projects as
the best protection against escalating construction costs. According
to information provided by the agency, the average implementation
period of a large sample of recent sub-projects ranged from nine to
twelve months (Table 3, page 23). The agency expressed concern with
the time lag and price escalation which may result from tendering works
under LCB in some specific situations.
14/ The exceptions noted concern: (i) a tendency to keep advertisement
time to the statutory minimum in ICB tenders and the generally
inadequate circulation-publication of bid invitations; (ii) the
possible re-evaluation of the financial, technical, equipment or
manpower capacities of previously pre-qualified bidders during the
review of tenders; and (iii) the possible rejection of the lowest bid
on the basis of unreasonably- low pricing.



- 19 -
3.14      Given the wide territorial distribution of sub-projects, such
agreement would have a useful and diffused impact on local procurement
practices in different parts of the country. Therefore FONHAPO would:
a)  undertake a formal commitment to enforce fully congruent LCB
procedures in operations financed under the loan (which would
represent its largest and more structured initiatives) while
strongly suggesting their adoption to all Sub-borrowers;
b) use force account procedures, up to a 21Z share of loan financed
works only in situations where their greater efficiency and
economic advantage over alternative methods can be reasonably
demonstrated, and will supply such justification with the
submission of the corresponding Sub-loan Agreements for Bank
approval.
3.15      The adoption of force account methods (administracion directa)
for execution of part of the infrastructure works (up to an aggregate
amount of US$50 million equivalent) would be consistent with the Government
policy of progressive devolution of construction, maintenance and operation
responsibilities to local utility corporations. Furthermore, low-income
housing projects are often financed as part of the Emergency Employment
P,lan (PEZUC) which supports deployment of labor-intensive procedures.
Finally in many municipalities the sub-borrowers contribute directly a
portion of the investment resources in kind, providing self-help or
contracted labor, off-site infrastructure, construction materials or land
tracts to be developed.
Loan Disbursement
3.16      No Bank disbursement is anticipated against the purchase of land,
or external infrastructure which are generally provided by the
sub-borrowers, nor against design and engineering fees or project
administration charges which are typical local costs shared between FONHAPO
and the sub-borrowers. The Bank would disburse only against civil works at
the rate of 65Z of expenditures. Disbursements in respect of expenditures
under construction contracts exceeding US$500,000 equivalent would be fully
documented. All other disbursements would be against statements of
expenditures. It is proposed to establish a Special Account at BANOBRAS on
the order of US$8.5 million, from which the Borrower would withdraw the
peso equivalent of the Bank's share of expenditures made on eligible
sub-projects according to the exchange rate in effect at the time
disbursements were made by FONHAPO. The Bank would further withdraw from
the Loan Account to replenish the Special Account pari-passu with the
submission of withdrawal documents by the Borrower. It was agreed that the
Bank would finance expenditures made by FONHAPO after January lst., 1985,
as eligible for retroactive financing up to the equivalent of US$8.5
million, provided that such disbursements were made in accordance with the
criteria and procedures stipulated in the Loan Agreement. December 31,
1992, is the Closing Date.
