Supervision and Monitoring
3.17      Given the territorial spread and the number of subprojects (at
least 100 would Include Bank financing), FOINHAPO would adequately staff
regional offices and comply with agreed supervision and monitoring
procedures limiting Bank personnel verification to intermittent control on
a sample basis. FONBAPO would forward to the Bank quarterly reports on
project progress and expenditures according to an agreed format. The
appraisal wission was satisfied that the supervision system applied by
FONHAPO is adequate (para. 4.07) and anticipates that Bank supervision
commitments on this project would not represent an extraordinary burden,
falling within the norm of 2-3 field mission per year. FONHAPO would
forward to the lank civil works contracts exceeding the equivalent of
US$500,000, which would be subject to ex-post review an a sample basis.
Review of contract documents would be carried out ex-post and ex-ante for



- 21 -
all contracts exceeding US$1.5 million equivalent. FONHAPO would continue
evaluating annually the impact of the project according to a program
satisfactory to the Bank, regularly forward to the Bank its monitoring
reports. The proposed key monitoring indicators are presented in
Table 5.24.
Accounting and Auditing
3.18      FONHAPO's accounts have been regularly audited by a firm of
independent public accountants acceptable to the Bank. The 1983 audited
report qualified the financial statements relating to the final disposition
of the old mortgage portfolio, an area of activity that win shortly become
immaterial to the financial statements as a whole (para. 5.04). With the
exception of the controls surrounding the collection of the old mortgage
portfolio, the accounting and internal controls are adequate. The records
are current and audit reports are presented on a timely basis. The
independent auditors would be requested to extend their normal audit scope
to include a special review of and a report on Bank financed disbursements.
The Special Account would be audited by BANOBRAS independent auditors.
IV.  IMPLEMENTATION ASPECTS
General
4.01      As already mentioned (para. 2.05), FONHAPO is structured in
several functional departments (gerencias).  Each of these departments
intervenes In different stages of the preparation and management of
subloans, according to the procedures established in FONHAPO's
Organizational Manual. The new structure of the agency is presented in
Annex II (Chart II-1). Charts IV-1 to IV-9 of Annex IV detail the specific
responsibilities of the various departments and their interactions. A
description of the cycle of subloan preparation and of the required
technical features of subprojects is given in the same Annex.
Planning and Budgeting (Charts IV-1 and IV-4 in Annex)
4.02      Every year, the Governuent appropriates to FONHAPO a certain
amount of resources, in the sector investment budget of SEDUE. Within the
month of September, FONHAPO's Planning Department, in consultation with
other departments, must submit a Program of Annual Operations which is
discussed first with SEDUE's budgeting authorities and then with SPP. The
budgetary request must specify FONHAPO's intended contribution to
investment through internally generated resources, the number and type of
units the PFnd proposes to finance during the year and the geographical
distribution of the credits under consideration. Eventual deviations of
the annual programs from the targets set In SEDUE's Medium Term Housing
Plan must be justified in light of the marketing experience acquired
(Monitoring and Evaluation Department), the current status of the pipeline
(Social Promotion Department), the operating results of the previous year
(Finance Department), as well as other aspects (Legal, Operations,
Technical Departments). The Planning Department also follow up on
implementation of the annual investment program, ensures its adherence to
sectoral objectives (as defined by SEDUE), and recommends to the General
Director any strategic changes that my be required.



- 22 -
Promotion (Charts IV-1 and IV-4 in Annex)
4.03      This is the main responsibility of the Social Promotion
Department, which makes the first contacts with potential Sub-borrowers.
The Department keeps the general public informed on FONHAPO's objectives
and procedures through field visits and the media, identifies opportunities
for local investment, carries out meetings with interested groups,
illustrates prototype technical solutions and financial schemes, and checks
out proposed project sites. It keeps a register of potential sub-borrowers
and holds the relevant information on file. Social Promotion operates in
close coordination with the Regional Delegations and can periodically
suggest to the Planning Department appropriate modifications to FONHAPO's
credit conditions based on the findings of direct surveys of the
socio-economic characteristics of target groups (para. 4.14).
Design Standards and Building Codes
4.04      The technical standards currently adopted In FONHAPO' s
subprojects (see Technical Notes in Annex IV) are economical for both
infrastructure and dwellings. Infrastructure specifications and levels of
service generally comply with the minimum requirements of local utility
companies, which do not deviate substantially from national regulations. In
the absence of local codes, FONHAPO has been adopting the mlnimum Federal
norms which are acceptable to the Bank. Dwelling design, layouts and
specifications mostly conform with SEDUE's tested prototypes, which are
generally cost-efficient and consistent Bank experience in low income
projects.
Progressive Construction and Self-help Activities
4.05      Different patterns of site development can be observed In the
ongoing sub-projects. In the first pattern, the beneficiary household
occupies its lot as soon as it is improved. The family erects a temporary
shack on the back of the lot, moves in, and undertakes the construction of
the house with more permanent materials on the front only later. In the
second pattern, a single-room, multi-purpose unit is built with durable
materials, in which the household finds shelter while it keeps adding on to
the construction. Finally, in other cases, the household continues to live
in the original rental premises elsewhere, until the unit in which it will
transfer is sufflciently 'complete".
4.06      The share of construction works carried out through self-help
labor also varies. In general it can be said that in 50% of the cases
the beneficiaries contract construction or improvement works to specialized
labor; for another 25%, there takes place a combination of self-help with
contracted labor; and only for the remaining 25% beneficiaries construct
the entire building themselves with little or no contracting of external
labor. FONHAPO estimates that a typical loan including 500 units,
generates about 216 temporary jobs over a period of 8 months, with an
average investment of Mex$80 millions.



- 23 -
Works Supervision (Charts IV-2 and IV-6 in Annex)
4.07      One critical role of FONHAPO's regional offices is to supervise
the progress and the proper execution of the works financed. The
Department of Operations Staff visits each sub-project monthly and records
the physical progress of the coostruction against the expected disbursement
profile. This information is reported to the Central Office on well
designed and useful forms, which permit close monitoring of the progress.
Implementation Periods
4.08      The construction  period of a typical Sites and Services
sub-project ranges from six to seven months (1983-1984 average for about
105 sub-projects). Tendering normally requires one to one and a half
months. Progressive Construction sub-projects generally take a little
longer, nine to ten months, with basically the same tendering period as
sites and services sub-projects.  FONHAPO's reports on physical progress
indicate that execution delays may take up to 20% of contracted
construction time. In its calculations, the appraisal mission adopted the
following average implementation schedules, starting at the tim the
sub-loan agreement is approved by PONHAPO's Loan Committee:
Table 3: Average Sub-project Implementation Schedules
Sites & Services   Progressive Construction
Tendering & Contract Award       1 to 1.5 months       1   to 1.5 months
Construction Q to Contract       6 to 7   months       9   to 10 months
Allowance for Delays             1 to 1.5 months       1.5 to 2  months
Implementation Periods           9 months average      12 months average
Advance Disbursement and Guarantees (Charts IV-2 and IV-6 in Annex)
4.09      Once a Sub-loan Agreement is approved by FONHAPO's Loan
Committee, and signed by the General Director, the Finance Department
issues and advance (up to 40% of the credit) to the sub-borrower who must
secure it by providing collateral guarantees. Sub-loan guarantees may take
the form of a real collateral, or of an affidavit ("aval") by the ttate
Government, which authorizes the Federal Authority (SUCP) to retain the
sub-loan amount from the fiscal participations of the State in case of
default by the sub-borrower. In addition the Sub-borrower must provide a
mortgage guarantee as well as pledge and trust guarantees.
4.10      The remainder of the sub-loan is disbursed on the basis of work
progress, as certified by the Department of Operations. The advance is
recovered proportionally from each successive payment. The final
disbursement is released upon the issuance by Operations of a certificate
of final acceptance of the executed works (para. 4.11). The Planning
Department keeps track of each disbursement made by Finance for control and
budgetary purposes.



- 24 -
Works Completion and Final Disbursement (Charts IV-2 and IV-6 in Annex)
4.11      When sub-project construction is finished, the Sub-borrower
applies for an inspection by FONHAPO's Operations Department which issues
the document certifying the satisfactory completion of all works (finiquito
de ejercicio fisico-financiero). Upon receipt of this document, Finance
pays off the sub-loan balance with a final check. The grace period thus
expires and the Sub-borrower initiates loan repayment to FONHAPO.
Allocation to Final Beneficiaries (Charts IV-2 and IV-7 in Annex)
4.12      An important feature of FONHAPO's operation is that sub-loans
become effective only after the sub-project facilities have been built and
a pre-identified group of buyers has been assigned the legal possession of
mortgaged premises by the Sub-borrower. FONHAPO controls this process
of allocation to the ultimate beneficiaries through the following steps:
a) Pre-selection: during the stage of sub-project identification, the
sub-borrower is required to carry out a study of the target group and
to produce a preliminary documentation with the assistance of FONHAPO's
Social Department Staff. A Cedula Socio-Economica must be compiled for
each beneficiary household and a tentative register of potential
beneficiaries is prepared  (Cuadro de Concentracion de la Demanda
Captada) which constitutes an integral part of the sub-loan
application. FONHAPO reviews and analyzes this profile of
socio-economic characteristics of the target group in view of their
family composition and the employment condition of the heads of
household and compares their paying capacity with the estimated unit
costs of the proposed sub-project.
b)  Allocation Plan:  prior to sub-loan approval, FONHAPO counsels the
co,munity organization involved and supervises the formulation of a
plan for the allocation of the serviced plots and dwellings to
individual beneficiaries: it controls the authenticity of the
information supplied by the borrower and verifies the legal validity of
individual household contracts. This plan must be updated as soon as
works reach the stage of 85% advance.
c)  Allocation Process:  Finally, the Social Department authorizes the
actual process of allocation after the final cost of the units is
determined and the financial a:rangements between the sub-borrower and
the beneficiaries are approved. It then releases a document upon its
satisfactory completion of allocation procedures.
Collection of Payments (Charts IV-3 and IV-9 in Annex)
4.13      The sub-borrowers recover sub-project costs from the final
beneficiaries through mortgage schemes, which include recuperation of
FONHAPO's subloan as well as other investment and administrative costs
borne by the Sub-borrower. Mortgages entail a 10% deposit and monthly
amortization payments up to the full amortization of loaned principal
amount. As part of its extension services, FONHAPO's staff advises the
sub-borrowers in setting out repayment schemes and collection programs.
This assistance covers three major steps:



- 25 -
a) Collection of Deposits: the program for collection of down payments
must be prepared as part of the sub-loan application. Actual
collection initiates at the nmment physical works achieve 85% advance
and is expected to be completed before the full credit amount is paid
out (upon FONHAPO's final acceptance of the works) and the repayment of
the subloan is undertaken; when necessary, the amount of down payments
is re-adjusted once the final price of the unit has been determined;
b) Collection of Monthly Payments: the sub-borrower must present to
FONHAPO a finalized plan for collection of mortgage payments from
beneficiaries as a condition for signing a sub-loan agreement; at this
moment suitable legal and financial arrangement must be in place,
including the specification of the legal remedies to be applied when
necessary;
c) Final Liberation of the Guarantees: before FONHAPO's Loan
Committee authorizes the final liberation of the guarantees and the
formal closing of the sub-loan (Finiquito Total de Credito), it
must be satisfied that the recuperation process has been
satisfactorily established.
Monitoring and Evaluation (Charts IV-3 and IV-9 in Annex)
4.14      The Monitoring and Evaluation Unit reports directly to
FONHAPO's management assessing the impact of the agency's programs (see
Annex VI). The reports are yearly and based on a stratified samples of
sub-projects and beneficiary households. They generally include the
following:
a) a statement of the evaluation criteria used in the survey, including
critical comments on particular aspects of the process;
b) the main findings of field visits and interviews, and a review of
the performance of specific lines of credits and sub-projects;
c) the evaluation of the impact of FONHAPO's lending program in specific
areas of the country; and
d) the global evaluation of FONHAPO's operations, products and impact.
V. 17INANCIAL ASPECTS
FONHAPO's Old Portfolio
5.01      Original Assets:  The original capital of FONHAPO as a separate
entity consisted of approximately Mex$8,448 million (Balance Sheet as of
December 31, 1981 in Table 5.1 of Annex V), and was composed for 20% of
cash and receivables, for 501 of a mortgage porcfolio, and for 30Z of land
and projects in process. FONHAPO inherited numerous administrative
problems connected with the latter assets. Many accounts due to FONHAPO
were from purchasers who withheld payments complaining that damages caused
by seismic tremors should be repaired. Almost immediately FONHAPO had to
start protecting the land properties against illegal invasions.



- 26 -
5.02      Land Properties:  The initial problems of title defense have been
substantially resolved and management currently maintains detailed records
of each property to periodically determine its highest and best use. The
financial projections accompanying this report assume all land will be sold
by 1992. The July 31, 1984 value of these assets was of Mex$3,360 million
(US$20 million).
5.03      The Original Mortgage Portfolio:  FONHAPO will incur losses
relating to the final collection of these accounts which at July 31, 1984,
were valued at Mex$3,411 million (about US$20 million). Unreliable lists
of payments prepared by BANOBRAS, the continued complaints of tenants,
costly repairs, and a standing FONHAPO's offer to deliver a clear title at
a 20% discount, all indicate potential losses. The Government recognizes
this fact and supports FONHAPO's efforts to divest these assets as soon as
possible by granting supplementary funds for the repair activities (Table
5.2). In practice, the Fund maintains separate accounts and a separate
administration (the Gerencia de Desarrollo Inmobiliario) for the activities
related to the original portfolio. Management expects to liquidate these
accounts within five years and a provision for losses has been recorded in
the financial projections in this report (Table 5.16). Not only will the
final disposition of these accounts relieve FONHAPO's management of an
administrative burden and clear the way for the new programs, it will also
remove all reasons for the external auditor's qualified opinion of the
financial statements for the last three years, which deals exclusively with
items related to the founding assets.
5.04      Relevance to Future Operations:  The possible defects in the old
mortgage portfolio should be viewed in relation to current and projected
activities. For instance, as of July 31, 1984, the investments in the new
program that began in 1982 were four times bigger than the old mortgage
portfolio (Table 5.3). Projected annual investment in the new programs for
1985, will be ten times the old portfolio. In 1985 the losses relaced to
-he old portfolio will be in the order of 3% of net income. The
cowlectibility of this old portfolio does not therefore emerge as a
significant issue in the appraisal of the loan program for 1985 to 1988.
TLe New Lending Programs and the Level of Government Support
5.05      For its new operations, FONHAPO depends heavily on Treasury
transfers. In 1983, this item represented 63% of the Fund's new
investments, which amounted to 7.2 billion pesos (about US$60 million at
the June 1983 controlled exchange rate). For the following year, SPP
initially authorized an investment budget of 8.8 billions pesos with
Federal transfers amounting to 54X of the total. However, the total
Government contribution was raised to 13.4 billion pesos with a
complementary allocation made in March 1984, reaching 75.1% of FONHAPO's
17.9 billion pesos of new sub-loans. Converted in June, 1984 dollars,
these represented about US$107 millions.15/ The 1985 Budget raised
Government transfers to 20.5 billion pesos (76.5% of total), to which
FONHAPO intends to add 6.3 billion of internally generated resources,
15/ See: FONHAPO, Programa Operativo Anual, 1984, p.25.



- 27 -
achieving an investment total of 26.8 billion pesos (approximately US$140
million in December, 1984 equivalent), which should result in the
completion of 69,000 units now in progress and the initiation of 98,000 now
ones.
5.06      Will the Government's fiscal effort to continue FONHAPO's
expansion be sustained indefinitely? The Medium Term Housing Plan set out
the ambitious target of reaching an annual production capacity of 200,000
units in year 1988. While the priority attached to the program by the
Administration is not in question, persisting inflation and fiscal
constraints justify some doubts as to the achievability of the original
targets.
5.07      Therefore the appraisal mission drew two alternative scenarios:
Scenario A, in which housing starts are levelled off at 100,000 units per
year with a gradual phasing out of budgetary transfers; and Scenario B, in
which the annual support of the Government is maintained constant Jin real
terms (the equivalent of US$100 million per year) and housing starts
achieve the expected volume of 200,000 by 1991. Both cases were analyzed
through a computer model (described in Annex V), in which a number of
assumptions are made as to the composition of FONHAPO's program and the
level of capital recovery in each sub-program. Before reviewing these
results. it is important to discuss the main features of FONHAPO's formulae
of capital recovery.
FONHAP0's Capital Recovery
5.08      Main Features:  As in all schemes of Mexican housing finance, the
monthly repayments of FONHAPO's loans are indexed to the variations of the
minimum wage. Moreover, the unit costs of the subproject facilities to be
financed must be contained within set multiples of the minimum wage
applicable at the moment a subloan is approved. Monthly charges are
established as a constant fraction of nominal wages and are inclusive of
interest and capital amortization. Loan maturity is flexible, depending on
the speed of inflation. Because the minimum wage rates are officially
adjusted to the accumulated value of inflation (once or twice a year,
albeit with some delay), this system has the merit of introducing some
adjustment in loan repayment, but also renders the prevailing credit
conditions in the housing sector difficult to comrare with those of other
sectors of the economy. A detailed description of FONHAPO's mortgage
instrument is given in Annex II para 4. The basic information is displayed
in Table 4. The amortization schedules for two typical subloans are given
in Tables 5.4 and 5.5 of Annex V.
5.09      As long as the minimum wage increases (in nominal terms) faster
than the applicable interest rate, most of the monthly payments go to
amortize outstanding principal, therefore shortening sub-loan maturity.
This rapid pattLen of disbursement and repayment is FONHAPO's chosen weapon
against escalating project costs and diminishing returns due to inflation.
In sum, the Fund's present financial scheme emphasizes the quick turnover
of capital and the relative affordability of housing solutions.

5.10      Compared to the average cost of funds (51.9% p.a. as of April
1985) FONHAPO's starting interest rates of 9% to 15% p.a. are markedly
negative. The gap is somewhat reduced over the life of the sub-loans. As
the starting rates are adjusted yearly by 1.1 per year, the impact of this
factor is rapidly compounded because interest charges are applied monthly
on the outstanding principal, which is reduced through amortization
payments only at the end of the year. The spread between nominal and
effective interest rates thus increases quite rapidly during the life of
the loan and helps capital recovery. For illustration purposes, assuming a
given pattern of inflation and corresponding adjustments of the minimum
wage, the correlation between nominal and effective interest rates on
typical FONHAPO sub-loans would result as follows:

5.11      Expected Capital Recovery: For the purpose of this report,
capital recovery is defined as the percentage of loaned principal amount
that is paid off over time by the discounted stream of payments made to
FONHAPO by the sub-borrower. To determine the present value of future
payments, assumptions must be made on annual inflation rates over the life
of the loans and eventual deviations from the forecasted values will have
to be monitored. This approach appears necessary to evaluate the Project's
mechanism for the amortization of sub-loans which, as explained above,
indexes monthly payments to the current value of minimum wages, which are
periodically adjusted to the accumulated level of inflation.16/
5.12      Future Inflation Assumptions:  Table 5.6 and Chart V-3 compare
the historical behavior of minimum wage rates (MW) with the consumer prices
index (CPI), the construction price index for social housing (INCEVIS), and
the average cost of funds to the banking system (CPP). Expectedly, the
correlation among these variables is not stable from year to year, but a
remarkable consistency can be observed in their trends over time. The
inflation projections adopted as basic reference in the appraisal of this
16/ While the "real' terms results of this approach are possibly more
reliable, they are not immediately comparable with the average targets
of "nominal' cost recovery on other housing projects of the Bank
which a 1981 staff survey reported to be the range of 2/3 of investment
costs. More recent evaluations, made in real terms, point to
considerably lower results.



- 30 -
project are deliberately conservative and assume a slow and gradual decline
from 1984 values of -30% per year. To gain awareness of what could happen
under alternative conditions, a more optimistic projection was also
outlined at -45% per year and a pessimistic one at -15% per year.
5.13      Sensitivity to Inflation:  Using a computerized model of the
typical amortization schedules for FONHAPO loans (Tables 5.4 and 5.5) the
mission tested the sensitivity of the capital recovery ratio (CRR) to
different evolutionary hypotheses of descending inflation. This was done
for: (a) the individual loans of different types and vintages; and b) for
the entire project portfolio assuming given shares and investment profiles
for different type loans.
5.14      Under plausible circumstances (Scenario A.1), in which inflation
rates are assumed to contract each year to 70% of the previous year's rate,
and that corresponding adjustments are made to the value of the minimum
wage, hence of the monthly payments, the typical sub-loan made by FONHAPO
would yield acceptable real terms recoveries. Under the same inflation
assumptions, the returns on the overall project including sub-loans of all
kinds and vintages show a small positive balance (Table 5.12). This
virtual surplus would become progressively larger (and capital recoveries
correspondingly higher) under less conservative assumptions of domestic
inflation.
5.15      The approach presented above assumes that the pace of inflation
continue to decline steadily over the life of the individual sub-loans,
down to a chosen cut-off rate of 9% in year 1990, after which inflation
would supposingly level. Alternative projections were made, assuming the
rate of inflation to remain constant throughout the life of the loans
(Chart V-4). Corresponding results are obviously less optimistic, as shown
in Table 5.08 and this comparative summary:

5.16      Other Sensitivity Tests:  Some complementary analyses were
carried out to determine the sensitivity of the ratio of capital recovery
to variations of: a) the starting rates of interest; b) the factor of
yearly adjustment of interest rates; and c) the percentage of down
payments. These tests were also performed under two conditions: (i)
assuming inflation to remain constant throughout the life of the sub-loan;
and (ii) assuming different scenarios of gradual decline of inflation.



- 31 -
5.17      The results, presented in Table 5.09 to 5.11 and in Table 5.7
afford the following conclusions:
a) the sensitivity of Capital Recovery Ratios (CRRs) to variations of the
starting rate of interest is obviously high in both circumstances;
b) the speed of annual adjustment of the interest rate is siglificant (for
the reasons explained in para. 5.10) but their Impact is limited by the
brevity of the amortization period which ranges between 45 and 125
months; and
c) the variations in the percentage of down payment are not affecting
significantly the real returns on the Investment; in an environment of
rapidly declining inflation they make little difference as soon as
inflation hovers around 30% per year and actually induce diminishing
results thereafter, but it should not be forgotten that down payments
constitute a critical factor of liquidity for FONHAPO at the present
time.
5.18      In conclusion, the most critical factor in FONHAPO's oulending
terms remains the level of the starting rate. Any modification aiming at
improving cost recovery should therefore concentrate on this variable.
Financial Prospects of FONHAPO
5.19       If the inflation assumptions of our Scenario A.1 (a  -30% decline
each year, with a 9Z cut-off), are accepted as reasonable, and supposing
further that FONHAPO's overall program would level off to a production of
100,000 housing starts per year (e.g. at half the original target level set
for 1988 by SEDUE's Housing Development Plan), the returns from the
agency's new portfolio should rapidly reduce the need for Treasury grants
to a tolerable level (about US$50 million equivalent by 1988) (Table 5.12
and Chart V-5). Albeit decreasing, budgetary support would still be needed
up to 1991 because of the following reasons: (i) the high level of erosion
experienced on capital loaned in the initial years; (ii) the deliberate
decision to keep repayment terms affordable by the target group; and
(III) the maintenance of a substantial level of investment.
5.20      A sensitivity analysis, based on a slower pace of inflation
decline (of -15% per year), and correspondingly lower adjustments to the
nominal value of minimum wages, produces the results shown in Scenario A.2
(Table 5.13). In this case, Government transfers would still be on the
order of US$70 million equivalent in 1988.
5.21      An altermative projection has been run (Scenario B), assuming
that the GOM should decide to maintain the present volume of budgetary
support to FONBAPO at US$100 million equivalent per year. Under similar
inflationary assumptions as in Scenario A.1, this would permit FONHAPO to
achieve indeed the planned production target of 200,000 housing starts
(in 1991) but, in this case, the Government would still be financing about
23% of the agency's investment in 1992 (see Table 5.14).



- 32 -
FONHAPO's Total Program
5.22       Only estimates of the actual volume of future government
transfers to FONHAPO are available. The Medium-Term Plan for Economic
Development identifies this program as a cornerstone of its social policy
and of the Emergency Employment Scheme, but no specific price tag is
attached to this recognized priority. The only figures available are those
originally proposed by SEDUE in the Mid-Term Housing Plan 1983-1988,
setting out the ambitious physical targets mentioned in para. 5.06, which
now may have to be reconsidered in light of persisting fiscal difficulties,
higher than expected inflation, competitive public expenditures
commitments, and the unpredictable flow of future revenues.
5.23      The only reliable information that can be obtained at this point
in time is the 1985 authorized budget which is for FONHAPO in the order of
Mex$26,800 million (US$140 million at the December 31 exchange rate), of
which MexS20,500 (US$107 million) constitute committed Government transfers
and Mex$6,300 (US$33 million) FONHAPO's own resources. For the years to
come it is safe to assume that fiscal transfers (including the Bank loan)
are unlikely to supercede the 1985 volume and that any incremental
expansion of the program would have to be financed out of FONHAPO's
increasing pc:tfolio returns.
5.24       In Scenario A, which was adopted as the basic reference for
project appraisal, it has been conservatively assumed that during the
sexenio the yearly production of FONHAPO would level off at 100,000 starts,
a volume that the agency might achieve as early as 1985 or 1986. Should
this become the case, the internally generated funds from FONHAPO's
portfolio would progressively reduce the need for Government support.
Treasury transfers would represent a diminishing percentage of total
investment and could eventually be phased out as the agency becomes
independently viable for a production of the stated, constant volumr   The
detailed analysis of FONHAPO's pro-forma financial statements (Balance
Sheets, Income Statements, Sources and Application of Funds, Government
Transfers and Annual Movements in the Mortgage Portfolio) presented in
Tables 5.15 to 5.19, has been limited to this perspective situation.
The Bank-Financed Project
5.25      In light of the uncertainty on the actual size of future
Government transfers, it is proposed that the Bank participation be limited
to a strategic, but circumscribed portion of FONHAPO's Investment Program
which we have defined as the Project. According to a seven year projection
of disbursement and to the conservative assumptions made above, this means
that the Project's expenditures (including the Bank loan and the
counterpart funding) will be on the order of 1/8 of the expected FONHAPO's
onlending in any given year (see Chart V-5). However, when related to
Government transfers, the Bank disbursement may amount to nearly 25Z of the
external support needed by FONlHAPO to maintain a production of 100,000
units over the next four-year period (1985-1988). In this respect, apart
from the foreign exchange benefit, Bank participation in the FONHAPO's
program would help significantly in relieving the Government short-term
fiscal burden. Tables 5.20 to 5.24 display the basic information
concerning the proposed project.



- 33 -
Financial Rates of Return
5.26      Financial rates of return (IRR's) have been calculated for the
Bank-financed project as a whole and separately for each of the two lines
of credit which would be supported with proceeds of the Loan. Furthermore,
IRR's have been computed for typical sub-loans of different vintages at
constant levels of inflation (Table 5.8) and under varying rates of
inflation decline (Table 5.7). Calculations have been made on the
discounted streams of payments (real IRR's) and on the nominal streams
(nominal IRR's).
5.27      The Project yields a positive IRR (above 10%) only if calculated
in nominal terms, but the IRR is expectedly negative (by a couple of
points) when calculated in real terms (an inequivocal indication of the
need of subsidy). With inflation slowing down during the life of the
sub-loans (by -30% each year), the real IRR improves from an average -14.3%
on loans made during 1985 to -0.8% on loans made in 1988, and would become
positive thereafter. Results vary between -15.5% to zero on Sites and
Services sub-loans and between -11.8% to +2.4% in Progressive Construction
sub-loans. If instead the calculations are repeated assuming inflation to
keep level, the IRR's are correspondingly lower as shown in Table 5.8.
5.28      These calculations assume the equity contributions to have
zero opportunity cost for FONHAPO, which is evidently not the case for the
GOM. A true measure of the amount of public subsidy involved in the
FONHAPO program would need to account for this as well as for other costs
implicit in the running of the Fund which are shown in the Projected Income
Statements (Table 5.19). The proposed joint annual reviews of FONHAPO's
performance and investment programs would include these considerations
(Annex V, para I-a).
5.29      The foregoing analysis has been developed to illustrate the
financial and fiscal implications of public intervention in low-income
housing credit, and to measure the extent of its replicability on a wider
basis. The project would include a broader study (Draft Terms of Reference
in Annex VIII) under which similar analyses would be performed for other
and weightier programs of Mexican housing credit with the intent of
achieving an integrated picture of the sector. It is in tbis comparative
context that the operations of FONHAPO are likely to emerge as a useful
indication for system-wide improvement.
VI. ECONOMIC ASPECTS
General
6.01      The main economic benefit of the project, gauged by its lasting
contribution to national income, would be the flow of rental value
generated by the additions to the housing stock. Reference could be made
to other benefits, most notably to the project's impact on the level of
employment during a situation of generalized underemployment of the
available labor supply. By FONHAPO's own estimates, an investment of one
million dollar equivalent in the production of Sites and Services would



- 34 -
generate approximately 500 direct jobs during 8 months of constructionl7/.
These other benefits are not quantified in this report. Likewise, in
regard to costs, the only measured cost used in the analysis is the market
value of investments in land acquisition, infrastructure, buildings and
maintenance18/.
6.02      -Two alternative methods have been adopted for the evaluation of
imputed rental values. The first one uses the traditional approach based
on current levels of rent (sale prices) paid by the household for
equivalent units after moving into the project sites. It is based on data
collected in six existing projects developed with FONHAPO's financing. The
projects were selected so as to represent the diversity of solutions
encompassed by the program. The other approach uses costs for a "typical"
lot (i.e. the averages for all sub-projects implemented in the first
semester of 1984) and rental values derived from current levels of rent
paid by the target population before moving into the sub-project as
reported in the 1984 FONHAPO Evaluation Sample (FES) (see Chapter VI
Annex).
Costs and Benefits: Market Value after Access to FONHAPO Sub-projects
6.03      Table 7 below summarizes the relevant information on the selected
projects. In all cases, the cost of land is based on actual market values
and the quoted price includes each lot's share of land donated for
infrastructure and services. Investment costs equal actual costs as
recorded by FONHAPO and --for the reasons discussed in Chapter VI Annex--
without adjustment for inflation, taxes or conversion factors. In the case
of Sites and Services the estimated rental value is based on equivalent
sites with some improvements, typically a non-durable structure without
services.
6.04      The resulting economic rates of return (ERR) are rather high --
in the range of 16% to 26% per year. Moreover, the sites and services
projects have systematically higher rates of return than projects involving
the production of semi-finished or finished housing19/. In theory the
ERR's based on rental streams should equal those based on sale-prices.  In
fact, for the examples at hand they differ - but by an acceptable small
margin. In four out of six cases, the ERR's calculated with the sale
prices exceed those estimated with rental values, but only in one case by
more than 30% (project 003).

construction as an instrument of employment generation, see: PRELAC:
El Efecto Empleo de la Inversion Publica, Santiago: Investigaciones
sobre Empleo No. 23 (1984), esp. pp. 40-49.
18/    See Chapter VI Annex for a discussion of the procedures used in the
cost/benefit analysis and for a definition of costs and time
horizon.
19/    Project 105 is an atypical example of home-upgrading (Vivienda
Mejorada) concerning a dilapidated tugurio in downtown Mexico-City.

Alternative Measurement of Benefits: Rents Paid before Access to Project.
6.05       In this alternative approach (based on current levels of rent
paid by the target population), we consider the aggregate sub-project data
for all renters in the FES sample.  As measure of the increasing
willingness of families to spend on housing of better quality, we raised
the percentage of the budget spent on housing by one standard deviation:
this laises the share from 16% to 24% (calculated on the basis of the head
of household's income alone). The higher market value of the new umits
would normally be associated with an impmovement in the average income of
renters. To capture this effect we increased the average income of the
head of househo.ld by one standard deviation, from Mex$23,785 per month to
Mex$36,244 (a rise of 52%). With these upward adjustments the rent payable
per room in the new housing units (in mid-1984 prices) is estimated at
Mex$4,367 per month.

6.06      Expectedly, there is considerable variation among the ERR's shown
in Table 8, which correspond to different sensitivity assumptions
concerning the costs and the benefits of the project. However, and this is
the important result, the range of values and the direction of this
variation are compatible with the figures in Table 7 which shows that Sites
and Services projects have higher rates of return than Progressive
Construction projects. Under the preferred set of assumptions (A, a) the
estimated ERR for a typical Sites and Services project is 21% (the range in
Table 7 is from 16% to 26%). For a Progressive Construction project the
race falls to 17% (with a range between 16% and 19%). The juxtaposition of
pessimistic assumptions concerning benefits (C) and costs (c) yields ERR's
of approximately 1OZ for either Sites and Services or Progressive
Construction projects. In sum, this alternative approach to the evaluation
of economic costs and benefits of a typical unit in the proposed project
supports the previous results based on actual market values for six case
studies. It confirms the conclusion that the project has high and
apparently stable rates of return.
VII. PROJECT RISKS
General
7.01      Several factors could have an adverse impact on the successful
implementation of the Bank financed project or its expected results. These
'concern:  (i) the capacity of FONHAPO's present structure to expand over
time and yet to maintain the present degree of operational efficiency;
(ii) the availability of land sites of adequate quality, quantity and price
to permit development of FONHAPO's projects; (iii) the consistency and
continuity of the Government's housing sector policy; (iv) the evolution of
the general conditions of the economy and the market; and (v) possible
delays or shortfalls in beneficiaries repayments to sub-borrowers, which
would raise the level of implicit project subsidy.
Growth Capacity of FONHAPO
7.02      Will FONBAPO be able to sustain a continued process of growth,
without loosing its present efficiency? Undoubtedly, further expansion of
its operations is desirable for the agency to make a significant impact on
the sector, but this expansion can be only as rapid as afforded by gradual
development of its institutional base and by the extent of financial
support it will obtain from the Government. At present, the Fund appears
to be well organized and staffed for the present scale of cperations. Some
significant structural changes have been introduced last year to accompany
the rapid expansion of the pipeline (i.e. the new organizational chart, the
creation of the regional offices, the adoption of a series of operational
manuals and of new financing rules). The growing volume of operations
requires new processing techniques, greater computerization of records, an
improved system of information and intensified technical assistance, and
supervision and monitoring of the projects' impact.



- 38 -
7. 03     Special care must be taken to Limit the agency's involvement in
sideline activities such as home upgrading, finished housing units, and
supplies of building materials, which demand staff deployment and resources
not always proportionate to their benefits (para. 3.10 a)). Similarly,
political pressures or the ambition of extensive geographic coverage,
should not prematurely dilute FONHAPO's operations into an excessive number
of small projects (para. 3.10 b)). The technical preparation of sub-loans
and the reliability of cost estimates and implementation schedules need to
be kept up to standard. Finally, the future financial viability of the
agency will greatly depend on the rational management of its resources and
their rapid turnover. All these potential issues have been addressed in
the desiga of the Bank financed project and are the object of corresponding
loan covenants.
Land-Sites Availability
7.04      Shortage of areas with suitable pricing and characteristics for
low income housing development has traditionally plagued housing policy in
Mexico. To some extent, the origin of this shortage is political and is
rooted in the perceived opportunity cost of available public land reserves
which tend to be allocated to "higher- (but not necessarily 'best") uses.
The present Administration has focussed early on this problem and has
attributed to SEDUE special powers for the priority utilization of
unutilized public property for low income housing. It has also made
available to SEDUE a limited budget for advance purchase of land reserves.
To the extent that these powers will be used to support FONHAPO's
operations, they could go a long way towards providing suitable project
sites at prices affordable by its target group. In addition, FONHAPO
itself makes available part of its resources to Sub-borrowers to finance
land acquisitions. While land purchases related to Bank-financed
sub-projects would not be the object of direct disbursement of loan
proceeds, they would be recognized in respect of cost sharing to the extent
of 11.6% of base costs (approximately US$35 million equivalent including
contingencies).
7.05      Admittedly, there is the possibility of speculative (windfall)
gains resulting from future extra-market constraints in the supply of urban
land for low-income housing, specially in sub-projects built on land
previously owned (or expropriated) by the Government. To neutralize these
gains (and not to further subsidize the original credit recipients), the
Bank should encourage FONHAPO (or its agents) to price all project-related
land at market values.
Possible Policy Changes
7.06      Continuity of Government support is deemed critical for the
future of FONHAPO.  This involves three main risks.  First, the Government
could find itself in a position that does not afford sustaining fiscal
allocations to FONHAPO at the scale planned. It must be noted in
this respect that the extraordinary expansion of the agency's activities
during 1984, was permitted by a "strategic" (i.e. complementary) allocation
made in the month of March, which nearly doubled the resources granted
under the ordfnary budget (para. 5.05). A sudden contraction in the annual



- 39 -
volume of funds transferred to FONHAPO would not cause grave concern for
the counterpart financing of the Bank project, since the latter represents
only a portion of the planned 1985-1988 investment program of the agency
(para. 3.07), but it would affect its public outlook as a rapidly expanding
financial agent and the morale of its staff.
7.07      Second, it is quite conceivable that, in the future, the
Government may seek to obtain alternative financing for the FONHAPO program
through the banking system (e.g. from FOVI-FOGA), or through the devolution
to FONHAPO of some of the deposits in the payroll funds (e.g. from
INFONAVIT) (para. 1.21). This might involve the need for some changes in
the rules of operations of FONHAPO and some shifts in product and target
population. Protective covenants in this respect are included in the legal
documents.
7.08      Thirdly, the onlending terms on FONHAPO's sub-loans (para. 5.08)
have been considerably hardened only last year. While it is probably too
soon to assess the full impact of these changes on the market, demand has
remained quite high, which may point to the fact that no alternative
sources of financing are available to the clientele of FONRAPO at present.
While new alternative credit windows could be opened by the Government,
possibly at more concessionary terms, this risk is mitigated by the high
level of commitment the Government has accorded to the FONHAPO program.
This consideration also underscores the fact that a certain gradualism must
be used in the introduction of measures to increase the profitability of
FONRAPO's operations, at least until the time the agency's activities had
reached fuller maturity and the paying capacity of its target population
had actually improved.
7.09      The latter point is critical, when one considers FONHAPO's
statutory objective of serving a large target population of very limited
personal income. The purchasing power of this group has been eroded by
inflation during the last years and the threshold of affordability of
Government-financed conventional housing slid up to higher and higher
income levels, to the extent that 70X of Mexicans became excluded from the
benefits of regulated housing credit. A reasonable measure of subsidy to
FONHAPO could be accepted as long as its production remains clearly
targetted to the low-earning groups (para. 5.19).
Inflation and Minimum Wages
7.10      As we have seen in Chapter V, FONHAPO's terms of capital recovery
are closely tied to the minimum wage policy. This may be justified in an
economy experiencing high rates of inflation, where wages are not
automatically indexed and their adjustments often lag behind the escalation
of prices (as is the case at present). Should the Government be successful
in progressively reducing the inflation rate (and we believe the
conservative expectations upon which this appraisal is based are realistic)
FONHAPO's current terms allow for acceptable rates of recuperation. If, as
can be expected, the Government should gradually up-grade real wages to
reapproach historical levels, this would have direct impact on construction
prices, but would not adversely affect FONHAPO's capital recovery since it
would also result in proportionately higher monthly payments. Only in the
case that construction costs should rise much faster than minimum wages,



- 40 -
would FONHAPO's present formula become vulnerable. However, this
circumstance could be offset by the introduction of additional charges for
interest during construction or similar adjustments. A protective covenant
involving joint review of the terms of cost recovery for the agency by the
Bank and the Guarantor is included in the Loan Agreement.
Other Risks
7.11      Two risks usuilly associated with low-income housing projects
have been substantially reduced by FONHAPO's past and current practice.
Because of its experience in the field, it is improbable that FONHAPO will
face technical difficulties in identifying affordable sub-projects for its
target population (see Annex VI). And there appear to be no danger that
the demand resulting from a country-wide shortage of housing could lead to
higher income groups obtaining the facilities targetted for tie lower
income population. These risks are reduced by the combination of FONHAPO's
procedures for pre-identification of credit recipients (who are not
individuals but organizations) which minimize delays in effective occupancy
of the improved sites and by the active role of other financial
intermediaries for the higher income brackets.
7.12      Finally, the project faces one additional risk, associated with
the wholesale character of FONHAPO's lending. There is the possibility
that beneficiary households delay payments or fail to pay the intermediary
agencies (i.e. FONHAPO's Sub-borrowers). Because many of these agencies
are well established and often governmental entities, this would not cause
default in payments to FONHAPO whose loans are properly secured
(para.4.09). But it would, of course, lead to an increase in the level of
final subsidy implied in the project. To reduce this risk, FONHAPO should
establish closer monitoring of the collection procedures of the
intermediary agencies and enforce legal action against unusual failures in
individual repayments.
VIII. ASSURANCES
8.01      During loan negotiations the following assurances were
obtained:
(a) The Government would carry out a Housing Policy Study, and
discuss the study's conclusions and recommendations not later than
December 31, 1986 (para. 1.25, 3.06 and Annex VIII).
(b) FONHAPO would prepare and forward to the Bank, for comment, not
later than December 31, 1985 a technical assistance program based
on an evaluation of sub-borrowers' requirements and carry out such
program not later than March 31, 1986 (para. 3.12).



- 41 -
(c) Not later than December 31, 1985, FONHAPO would forward to the
Bank for its comments a program to monitor the economic, financial
and social impact of sub-projects. This program would be adopted
not later than March 31, 1986. The preliminary results and values
of agreed monitoring indicators wculd be forwarded to the Bank
beginning March 31, 1986 and annually thereafter. FONHAPO would,
by December 31 of each year, forward to the Bank for review and
comment, the proposed investment program for the following fiscal
year as well as the final evaluation of activities during the
previous fiscal year (para. 3.17 and 4.14).
(d) FONHAPO would, by December 31, 1985, furnish to the Bank for its
comments tbe results of a study of FONHAPO's medium term financial
viability. The recommendations of the study, would be adopted not
later than December 31, 1986.
(e) FONHAPO would adopt the Statement of Policy (condition of
effectiveness) (para. 7.07 and Annex II, para. 4).
(f) BANOBRAS would enter into contractual arrangements with the
Government providing for the transfer of loan funds to FONHAPO as
equity contributions of the Government and repayment of the loan
by the Government (condition of effectiveness).
(g) FONHAPO would calculate expected capital recovery on a yearly
basis for typical sites and services and progressive construction
subloans and would forward results to the Bank by March 31 of each
year. If in any year capital recovery, calculated in accordance
with agreed assumptions, falls below 50%, FONHAPO would take
appropriate measures to increase capital recovery to such level.
(h) The Government would monitor FONHAPO's economic cost recovery on
the basis of a formula accertable to the Bank and to exchange
views on the results of this calculation during annual reviews of
the FONHAPO program (para. 5.28).
8.02      With the above assurances and conditions, the project is suitable
for a Bank loan of US$150.0 million to BANOBRAS for a period of 15 years,
including a grace period of three years at the standard, variable interest
rate.

LOW INCOME HOUSING PROJECT
FONHAPO's NEW OPERATIONAL STRUCTURE
1.        The statutory objectives of FONHAPO are as specified below:l/
i)    to finance the following schemes envisaged in the National
Housing Program: (1) land reserves; (2) sites and services; (3)
progressive construction dwellings; (4) home upgrading; (5)
complete dwellings; and (6) production and distribution of
building materials;
ii)    to finance housing projects of agencies of the federal, state and
municipal governments, and of cooperative associations and
organizations legally constituted to build low-income housing;
iii)    to finance the purchase and construction of rental dwellings and
residential estates;
iv)    to request, receive and purchase land of any type of tenure; to
sell such land in order to obtain resources for financing
low-income housing and to constitute FONHAPO's own land reserves
for its purposes;
v)    to promote, support, and develop mechanisms which lower the cost
of housing construction inputs;
vi)    to make loans for the acquisition, construction or upgrading of
low-income housing;
vii)    to make loans for the supply of building materials, supporting
low-income housing construction;
viii)    to discount, re-discount or give out to be re-discounted credit
titles corresponding to operations consistent with the Fund's
objectives;
ix)    to guarantee the amortization and the payment of interests on
titles or certificates which may be issued through other credit
institutions in order to obtain resources to implement the Fund's
objectives;
x)    to obtain loans in national currency from whatever credit source
in accordance with the relevant legislation, ensuring that the
interest rate and other charges on its active lending operations
shall cover the payments due on its passive lending operations;
1/ Acuerdo con el que se autoriza la creacion del Fideicomiso Fondo de
Habitaciones Populares, Diario Oficial de la Federacion, April 2, 1985,
Mexico D.F. and Contrato de Fideicomiso Fondo de Habitaciones
Populares, of May 13, 1981, between SHCP, BANOBRAS and SABOP.



-44-
CHAPTER II ANNEX
Page 2
xi)    to promote and participate in research, technical or other
activities, related to the provision of low-income housing;
xii)    to support other activities of information, support,. advice,
study and promotion for the provision of low-income housing;
xiii)    to promote in general the improvement of the residential
conditions of population groups with lesser incomes.
2.        The sources of financing of FONHAPO are listed below:
i)    the active patrimony of the pre-existing Fondo de Habitaciones
Populares, according to the audited statements of December 31,
1980 (see para. 5.01 and Table 5.3 in Chapter V Annex);
iI)    the contributions of whatever type by the Federal Government,
Federative Entities (the States and the D.F.), Municipalities and
other public or private institutions;
iii)    the resources generated by direct investments of the Fund;
iv)    the income obtained from the transferred equity capital;
V)    whatever other contribution and flxed or movable assets which the
Fund may acquire under whatever legal title.
3.        The rules of operation of FONHAPO include the following:
i)    ensure the consistency of its lending programs with policies of
national/regional development and with local demographic,
economic and social characteristics, giving priority to
sub-projects located in areas or centers specified by the
Government;
ii)    allocate no more than 1OX of its resources to the Federal
District in the geographical distribution of funds;
ii')    require Sub-borrowers to supply appropriate social, legal,
technical and financial justification for each sub-loan
application, and demand a study specifying the characteristics of
final beneficiaries;
iv)    revise the marketing studies and financial projections prepared
by Sub-borrowers to ensure the economic feasibility of the
planned operations;
v)    ensure that sub-projects meet appropriate architectural and
planning standards and minimize the cost of the housing units;



-45-                    CHAPTER 11 ANNEX
Page 3
vI)     require updating of sub-projects investment costs (prices must be
adjusted to one month prior to sub-loan signature) and include
price escalation in the loan amount so as to minimize need for
later complements;
vii)    achieve the best possible combination of local contributions and
own financing;
viii)    supply technical assistance and management advice to
sub-borrowers for the preparation and processing of their
projects, and supervise works, providing consultants selected
from a roster and financing if necessary for these purposes;
ix)    require full documentation of disbursement of sub-loan proceeds
and request auditing of expenditures for works, supplies and
services;.
x)    review and approve sub-loan applications through a Technical
Comittee for the Distribution of Funds on the basis of
standardized procedures and documentation.
4.        Terms and Conditlons for FODHAPO credits (Table 4 and para. 5.08)
were revised In March, 1984, as follows:
i)    Cost Sharing:  Sub-borrowers must make a direct contribution to
the cost of investment, either in cash or In kind (e.g. land,
infrastructure, materials, labor); in practice FONHAPO finances
from 75 to 95 percent of total investment costs, depending on the
line of credit and/or the characteristics of the Sub-borrower;
ii)    Sub-loan Amount:  established in function of the cost of the
units to be produced, expressed in multiples of the daily minimum
wage prevailing ID the geographical zone where the sub-project is
located at the time the sub-loan is signed; there is a specified
range of units costs for each line of credit and a maximum
ceiling of 2,000 times the applicable minimum daily wage (In
January 1985, this unit cost ceiling was equivalent to US$9,364);
III)    Down Payment:  a*1  FONEAPO's sub-loans, except thowe for building
materials, require down payments of an average IOZ of the
principal amounts, which Is deposited at the begluning of the
financing period, one month after certification of works
completion;
Iv)    Monthly PayMents: established as a fixed proportlon of the
applicable miLn  um wage at the time of each payment (end of the
mw'th) and lncreased over time as the minimum wage is adjusted to
inflation; the percentage is established by PONHAPO in function
of the costs of the units;



-46-
CHAPTER II ANNEX
Page 4
v)    Interest Rates: charged at the end of each month on outstanding
principal amount; starting rates vary for the different lines of
credit and are adjusted annually by a factor of 1.1;
vi)    Amortization of Principal Amount: computed on a yearly basis:
thus the portion of monthly payment accruing to capital
amortization is smaller than it should be, while the balance
represents interest payments; this result is compounded by the
yearly adjustment of interest rates and creates over time a
significant differential between nominal and effective annual
rates (see para 5.10, Table 5);
vii)    Maturity: the maximum amortization period is established in 20
years; however, rapid inflation in the nominal level of monthly
payments may result in much shorter periods of loan repayment
(presently they vary between 3.5 and 7 years);
viii)    Loan Insurance:  there are two types of mandatory loan insurance
covering 75% of outstanding principal amount: one on the
amortization of the loan, and one against exceptlonal events;
ix)    Collateral: a sub-loan must be secured by a fiscal guarantee from
the Government of the State in which the sub-projects are located
or by other securities to the satisfaction of FONHAPO;
x)    Penalties for Arrears:  a charge of 5X per month is levied on
delayed payments; FONEAPO reserves the right to foreclose
sub-loans after a 90 days delay.

TECHNICAL NOTES
Preparation of Sub-projects
1.        Once the interest of a potential sub-borrower in receiving a loan
is ascertained by FONHAPO, the next step consists in the development of the
studies necessary for the preparation and the execution of a credit
contract. Four phases can be distinguished:
a)  Identification of Sub-projects and Sub-loan Application:   this
includes the promotional efforts and advisory services described
in para. 4.03 of Chapter IV. During this phase, a first
documentation on the sub-project is compiled on the basis of a
standardized format. Various Departments of FONHAPO are called
upon to give their opinion on the technical, operational,
financial, legal and social aspects of the sub-project. Once the
favorable opinion of each is obtained, the application is
considered viable and the sub-borrower is instructed to proceed
with actual preparation.
b)  Feasibility Studies and Preparation of Sub-loan Documents:  this
second phase consists in the preparation of the sub-project
documents (technical, financial, legal and social). Preliminary
engineering design and studies are prepared. Occasionally, FONHAPO
makes a project preparation loan to cover the costs of surveys and
engineering drawings,/ but in the majority of cases, these costs
are borne by the sub-borrowers.  This process is iterative, and
may require two or three consultations with FONHAPO's
professionals. The legal aspects of land tenure, and the
Sub-borrower's criteria for selection of ultimate beneficiaries are
also preliminarily reviewed. This phase ends with the
authorization of the credit by FONHAPO's Loan Committee, which is
based on the amount estimated through preliminary costing plus an
allowance for physical and price contingencies.
c)  Final Engineering and Sub-borrowers Commitments:  this phase lasts
from 35 to 45 days, during which time the engineering drawings,
cost estimates and sub-loan documents are finalized. FONHAPO's
technical personnel then makes a field visit, to ascertain the
1/ Since the fund requires that engineering documents and working drawings
be signed by a professional, FONHAPO maintains a roster of qualified
consultants which it makes available to sub-borrowers on request. The
roster includes specialists in Urban Design, Architecture, Equipment,
Infrastructure, Building Site Organization, Construction Supervision,
Cost Estimates and Legal Services.



-48-               CHAPTER IV ANNEX
Page 2
congruence of the design to the topography and soil characteristics
of the site, its present land use and tenure situation, and the
availability of off-site infrastructure. At the same time, the
sub-borrower's representatives are instructed on FONHAPO's
supervision, monitoring and disbursement practices. It is during
this phase that a formal commitment is obtained from the
sub-borrower and the local utility companies for the provision of
off-site infrastructure, if this is not already available.
Similarly, the tenure situation of the proposed land-site is
subject to verification. In all cases, one of FONHAPO's conditions
for granting the credit is that the sub-borrower must produce clear
title for the subproject site.
d) Sub-Loan Agreement: the Sub-loan Agreement is formalized between
FONHAPO's General Director and the Sub-borrower, only after the
project documentation is completed in all its details and has
received satisfactory review by all concerned departments of the
Fund. To ensure compliance with effectiveness conditions, the
Legal Department pays a visit to the sub-borrower. The planning
Department makes the relevant budgetary controls. The Loan
Agreement is stipulated according to a standardized form, which has
been reviewed by the Mission and found acceptable. FONHAPO
requires that a contingency allowance be included in the loan
amount and discourages the practice of subsequent adjustments to
the loan amount for cost overruns. However, in those cases where
an adjustment to the original terms and conditions of a contract is
justified (whether due to changes in specifications, number of
solutions or loan amount) a formal application must be made and
meet with the approval of the Operations, Finance, Legal and Social
Departments. Prior to the stipulation of a Revised Loan Agreement,
the sub-project must be re-programmed in all its aspects.
Extension Services
2.        FONHAPO's own staff contribution focusses on two Stages of the
development of a sub-project. In the preparatory stage, it assists in the
creation of a Support Committee (when no social organization exists as yet)
or provides advice to an existing organization (for the establishment or
strengthening of a suitable technical cadre). During a second stage, that
of administration of the credit, FONHAPO's staff meets periodically with
the Support Committee to accompany and guide its relation with the
sub-borrower during the various phases of loan processing, and for
obtaining the services and infrastructure envisaged in the Loan Agreement.
Although FONHAPO is basically a financial rather than an implementation
agent, it considers extremely important to set up the initial structures of
the Support Committees, so that they can eventually develop into larger and
effective community organizations within which the future residents can
supervise and manage the credit themselves, solve all problems typical of
the settlement and promote social, cultural, and recreational activities.



CHAPTER IV ANNEX
-49-                     Page 3
Technical Aspects
3.        Land Availability.  In the =.Iority of cases the sub-borrower
already owns the land. Quite often, FONHAPO's financed sub-projects are
implemented on areas which had been acquired in the past by the State
Housing Institutes or by Federal housing agencies like INDECO.2/ In other
cases the site is acquired On the market by sub-borrowers and only rarely
it is the product of expropriation or re-use of ejido property. The lack
of adequate planning in the past account for the fact that generally, the
sites available for FONHAPO's programs tend to be located at the periphery
of cities, sometimes separated from the presently urbanized areas, with
poor access roads and scant, if any, urban services. ID time, these
problems will be lessened as the cities expand and the areas where
FONHAPO's settlements took place become mDre accessible and better
serviced. In order to facilitate the purchase of suitable sites, FONHAPO
makes available sub-loans for this purpose.
4.        Lot Sizes and Land Use:  The average lot size on the sub-projects
fin.tnced by FONHAPO during 1983 and 1984, was of 117 m2, with a modal range
of 60 to 200 m2. This plot size, which applies to both sites and services
and progressive construction sub-projects, is in line with recommended
standards and similar experiences of the Bank. Typically, some 2/3 of the
land sites are salable to individual households. About 10% of the gross
area is reserved for community facilities (schools, health and social
center&, recreational areas) which are improved through various ongoing
sectoral investment programs. The corresponding portion of the sites is
generally donat.d to the corresponding authorities. The rest constitutes
rights of way which are turned over to the municipalities.
5.        Infrastructure Standards:  As mentioned above, one of the
requirementi for credit approval is the formal commitment by the
sub-borrower to provide the needed off-site infrastructure, complying with
the specifications and levels of service prescribed by the public utility
companies. FONHAPO finances only on-site infrastructure, which generally
include the following:
i) streets and pedestrian walkways: levelling and compacting of
sub-grade for the whole roadway width, concrete curbs and unpaved
sidewalks; in some cases a compacted base course is laid out, with
or without asphalt seal spray (asphalt pavement is rarely
provided);
2/ INDECO, the National Institute for Housing and Community Development was
a public low-income housing agency, active from 1970 to 1982. After
1982, its activities were incorporated in the FONHAPO program. Its
actions included slum rehabilitation and the acquisition of land
reserves for sites and services and urban upgrading. INDECO financing
was often obtained through bridge loans granted by the FOVI program.
The conditions of the loans were similar to those granted by the PFV for
the least expensive housing type (VAIN). The total output of INDECO
during the last sexenio was on the order of 130,000 units.



-50-
CHAPTER IV ANNEX
Page 4
ii) drainage of surface waters: open gutters are used as much as
practicable, with concrete pipes only when strictly necessary;
iii) sanitation: some type of sanitation is always provided, usually in
form of concrete-pipe waterbcrne sewerage with individual household
connections;
iv) fresh water: PVC is the material mostly used in the networks, which
always include household connections; ground reservoirs,
watertanks and electrically driven pumping wells when necessary;
and
v) electricity and public lighting: aerial distribution with copper
conductor wire and pole-mounted transformers are mostly used for
low tension networks; the same poles are used for the installation
of public lighting lamps.
6.        Shelter Standards.  The line of credit for progressive
construction dwellings (Vivienda Progresiva) provides funds for the
erection of dwelling units as well as for implementation of the
corresponding infrastructure facilities. The average size nf a start-up
unit, in subprojects initiated during 1983 and 1984, was of 38 m2 with a
modal range of 27 m2 to 42 =2. The covered space is generally divided into
a living room, one or two bedrooms, a kitchen and a bathroom. Construction
techniques are generally labor-intensive, typical of the regions in which
sub-projects are built. Materials also tend to be traditional: stone
masonry and concrete for the foundation works; bricks, cinder blocks or
wood for the walls; and concrete, asbestos, or zinc plate for the roofs.
Design layouts are elementary, mostly conforming with well tested
prototypes supplied by SEDUE, which in general result satisfactory.
