-61-                CHAPTER V ANNEX
Page 1
MEXICO
LOW-INCOME HOUSING PROJECT
A.  DESCRIPTION OF THE COMPUTER MODEL USED FOR THE FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
General
The financial analysis, projections and statistical tables
presented in the repor-. were produced with a computer model that uses LOTUS
1-2-3 on an IBM-PC or compatible equipment with a minimum of 512K memory.
The modules are fairly complex and require approximately 200 KB of memory.
The model consists of three distinct modules. The first, the
amortization module, is a simulation of PONHAPO's lending practices, which
producea a monthly amortization table of a given FONUAPO loan together with
accompanying statistics such as the length of amortization, the internal
rates of return and the percentage of capital recovery, based on either the
nominal or the discounted cash flows.
The second module, the program and investment module, produces a
projection of FONHAPO lending activity from 1984 to 1992 and a forecast of
cash revenues from the mortgage portfolio which can be expressed In current
Mexican pesos or in constant 1984 dollars.
The third module, the financial statement module, produces
projected balance sheets and income statements from 1984 to 1992 and
contains historical financial statements since the founding of FONUHAPO in
1980.
Model Flexibility and Future Use
The user inputs provide for flexibility, allowing to change many
assumptions such as interest rate and relation of minimum wage to
inflation, loan size, etc. With a modest amount of additional work, the
underlying assumptions built into the tables of FONHAPO's loan conditions
such as the percentage of miniwmu wage devoted to mDnthly payments and the
starting interest rates may be changed as well. Also the model can
incorporate actual inflation rates or user supplied projected an annual
inflation rates for each disbursement year.
The basic conceptual characteristics of the model are described
in the following pages, Use of the model as it stands requires a person
familiar with LOTUS 1-2-3, although with additional work the model could be
made more 'user-friendly" for deployment by financial analysts with no
prior computer experience and could become a useful tool to unify World
Bank and FONHAPO's criteria in monitoring loan activities.
I.    Amortization Module
The amortization module simulates PONHAPO' s lending practices and
produces a monthly amortization table. A description of current FONHAPO's
lending practices is contained in paras. 5.08 and 5.09 of the report and



-62-                    CHAPTER v ANNEX
Page 2
will not be repeated here other than to mention that the interest rate and
monthly payment vary each year and are dependent on the original amount of
the loan and the periodic changes in the minimum wage . To calculate
discounted cash flows and expected levels of current peso lending activity,
the model incorporates inflation assumptions supplied by the user for the
years 1984 to 1995.
a.  User Input
The model requires the user to input the following information:
Inflation rate in 1984
Annual rate of change in the inflation rate
The minimum (cut off) inflation rate
Start year
Financed amount, expressed in multiples of minimum wages
Relationship between inflation and the minimum wage.
b. Computer Supplied Input to the Amortization Table
Annual inflation rate 1984 to 1985 based on user inputs of inflation
rate in 1984 and projected changes.
Size of the loan, expressed in multiples of minimum wages (escalated
by the computer supplied inflation rates).
Disbursement schedule, based on the current FONHAPO practice of
advancing 40% of loan upon effectiveness followed by an 11 month
disbursement period which is the current average disbursement period
for a typical loan.
From a data table incorporated in the model the computer supplies the
loan amount and the percentage of down payment, the starting interest
rate corresponding to the origina'. size of the loan, the yearly
percentage increase in starting interest rate, and the percentage and
amount of monthly payment adjusted yearly for the change in the
minimum wage.
c.  Output:  Amortization Table (see Tables 5.4 and 5.5)
The model calculates a monthly amortization table showing monthly
payments of capital and interest until maturity. In addition, the
amortization table calculates a cash flow discounted by the annual
projected inflation rate compounded monthly.
d.  Output:  Financial Indicators (Table 5.7)
Upon completing the amortization table, the model calculates the
internal rate of return and the capital recovery ratios based on the
nominal or discounted cash flows as well as the number of months of
amortization.
In addition, the model calculates the factors of annual capital
amortization and interest charges to be used in the program and
investment, and in the financial statement modules.



-63-
CHAPTER V ANNEX
Page 3
By incorporating the projected number of units to be financed each
year, a data table will supply levels of investment and the
corresponding statistics over the disbursement period of the World
Bank loan in order to evaluate the capital recovery on the project
and on the program as a whole (see Module II, below).
The Bank financed project contemplates participation in two of
FONHAPO's programs, sites and services and progressive housing, each
with distinct terms as to starting interest rates and level of
monthly payments. Consequently, the project data evaluation prepares
the key statistics of each component and combines them in a
consolidated analysis.
f. Sensitivity Analyses (Tables 5.7 to 5.11)
The module allows to produce a series of tables based on selected
values of all the basic inputs (starting inflation rate, rate of
annual charge in inflation, cut off rate of inflation, start year for
the loans, financed amount, relation between inflation and minimum
wage, percentage of down payment and pace of annual change in the
interest rate). It also allows to derive rates of return and capital
recovery ratios for loans of different types (sites and services or
progressive housing) and vintages (start years) and combines results
to analyze returns on the total project portfolio under varying
conditions.
g.  Interpretation of Key Indicators
The most critical indicator of FONHAPO's operation is, of course the
capital recovery of the loan itself. It is recognized that any
corrective measure to improve recovery would involve changes in the
basic loan conditions. Other cost factors, such as the complementary
share of investment directly contributed by the sub-borrowers or the
general administrative expenses of FONHAPO are not treated in this
model, but have been considered in the formulation of the project's
total costs (see Project Costs Table on page vi).
It is also recognized that the cost recovery levels of the loan terms
are discounted only for inflation and do not account for the
opportunity cost of the capital transfers that FONHAPO as a public
agency receives In form of equity contribution from the Central
Government. The model has been conceived for eventual use by FONHAPO
itself and is strictly intended to produce indicators for internal
financial control.
I-a.      Economic Variant of the Amortization Module
The Program Division has studied a variant to the Amortization
Module. This consists in the introduction of a formula-derived measure of
the opportunity cost of capital, which would permit to express the returAS
on FONHAPO's subloans in terms of market or shadowr costs (i.e. in an
economic mode). On the other hAnd, by zeroing the values of the variabies
introduced, the computer falls back into its unmodified version and
produces the original results (i.e. in the financial mode). The formula
proposed by the LClMX economist consists in the following:



-64-                    CHAPTER V ANNEX
Page 4
(1) Balace Sheet Identities: I - L - D
(whe ..- _eo _=zh; L - loas; D - debts , deposits)
Zera-Profit- Coaditio&4--
(2) R (1 -a); - D   - %.R + L.CI             from UVich
(3)  R- m iD + R[rkT + CZ                    where
(4)  iD   (I + Rd) ( + dP) -                 and
(5) t - X (by definition)
The following parameters would be specified by the model user:
Rd = real opportunity cost of loanable funds
Rk = required rate of return on equity
1 - equilibrium debt-equity ratio
Cl - intermediation cost per loan unit
a = rate of default and arrearage
dP = inflation rate
The economic discount factor in equation 3 is simply equal to the social
rate of return to capital (Rk) when the debt-equity ratio is zero (the
intermediary does not borrow, it only receives equity or transfers),
provided that the cost of intermediation is zero and the rate of default
and arrearage is also zero. Alternatively, if intermediation costs and
default and arrearage are zero, and if the debt-equity ratio is infinite
(the intermediary funds all its lending by borrowing), than the discount
rate is simply the opportunity cost of loanable funds (equation 4). The
latter becomes equal to the (expected) rate of inflation if the real
opportunity cost of loanable funds (Rd) is zero. Hence the financial (cash
management) mode of the model is seen as a special limiting case of
economic mode.
II.       Program and Investment Module (Tables 5.12, 5.13, and 5.15)
This module imports the amortization statistics, for different
loan-sizes escalated by inflation and produces a projected level of loan
placements during the estimated seven-year (1985 to 1992) disbursement
period. By incorporating the cash flow from the mortgage portfolio and
other estimates of miscellaneous incomes such as supervision fees and
income from land sales the module estimates the level of Government
transfers necessary to finance the projected level of activity.
a. User input
Inflation assumptions used in the amortization module.
Projected units of investment during the seven year disbursement
period 1985-1992 by line of credit (sites and services, progressive
housing and home improvement loans).
Estimates of miscellaneous income.



-65-                  CHAPTER V ANNEX
Page 5
b.  Output
Average loan size and investment for each line of credit and for each
disbursement year (the model assumes FONHAPO stops loan operations in
1992, the last disbursement year, in order to evaluate post
disbursement portfolio recovery).
Yearly and accumulated cash receipts.
Yearly and accumulated cash deficit (Government transfers) or surplus
during the disbursement period.
Post disbursement cash receipts from the mortgage portfolio to
maturity in 1998.
Percentage of Government transfers required to finance the projected
loan activity.
III.      Financial Statement Module (Tables 5.1 to 5.3 and 5.15 to 5.19)
This module imports the project investment data together with the
cash flow projections and converts it into projected balance sheets,
statement of results of operations, a statement of yearly and accumulated
Government transfers and a statement of sources and uses of funds. The
premises used in these projections are detailed in Chapter V Annex B ofthis
report.
a.  User input
User supplied premises relating to miscellaneous income and expenses.
b.  Computer input
Levels of investment and cash recovery from the program and
investment module.
c.  Output:  1985 to final maturity in 1998
Balance sheet (Table 5.15)
Income statement (Table 5.16)
Statement of source and uses of funds (Table 5.17)
Statement of yearly and accumulated Government transfers
(Table 5.18).
d.  Interpretation of output
It is interesting to note that FONHAPO's operating expenses represent
approximately 3.5% of loan placements and are substantially covered
by its miscellaneous income from other sources. Barring anomalies in
the pattern of expenses or significant changes in the nature of its
operations as a wholesale mortgage lender, monitoring of FONHAPO's
operations can be reduced to close analysis of the discounted capital
recovery results from lending activities simulated in the
amortization module.



-66-           CHAPTER V ANNEX
Page 6
B. PRINCIPAL ASSUMPTIONS UNDERLYING THE FINANCIAL PROJECTIONS
Income projections
1. Loans under the new bousing program
a. All FONHAPO loans to sub-borrowers are guaranteed by the Mexican
federal government.   Consequently, the projections carry no
provision for uncollectible accounts.
b. The interest rates charged are based on the current FONHAPO
regulations (Politicas de Administracion Crediticia y Financiera,
page 11). Based on the average size loan for each program as of
July 1984, the projections contain the following rates:
Starting   Annual
Rates     increase
Program
Sites and Services               9%          10%
Progressive Housing             11%          10%
Rome Improvements                9Z          10%
The loan terms provide for a 10% down payment and the nonthly
payments vary each year according to inflation assumptions. These
variables have been extensively analyzed in computer mDdels
prepared by Bank staff.
c. Amortization and construction costs
The amortization of FONHAPO project loans include the following
assumptions:
Inflation starts in 1984 at 60Z and decreases at the rate of 30%
per year down to a minimum of 9%.
Minimnm wages increase annually by a percentage equal to the
inflation of the previous year.
The monthly payments increase each year in the same proportion as
the increase in minimum wages.
Construction costs increase annually by a percentage equal to the
current year's inflation; on the average, 67% of construction costs
are incurred in the start year with a carry-over of 33% (at the new
costs) in the following year, except in 1992, the last year of the
program, when all costs of 1992 starts are incurred in 1992.
The down payment is received in the last month of the start year
and mDnthly payments begin the first month of the following year.



CHAPTER V ANMnx
-67-         Page 7
2. Interest income and expenses
a.  Short-term investments
Under current bank regulations FONHAPO may borrow funds at
lower-than-market rates but receives market rates on any short-term
deposits. In 1984 FONHAPO's short-term borrowings costs were 152
and interest paid on short-term deposits is approximately 30Z. The
financial management of FONHAP0 proudly asserts that all
administrative costs are covered by this difference. As inflation
decreases, however, this margin between interest Income and
interest costs will decrease. The projections show this Income
decreasing from Mex$ 1,116,079 (72Z of net income) in 1984 to
Nex$ 189,000 (.5% of net income) in 1992.
b.  Constructlon funds
FONHAPO's financial management estimates that the interest income
earned on government transfers received before they are needed for
disbursements to sub-borrowers approximates 2.1Z of construction
costs.
3. Construction income
Management estimates its income for supervision projects will
approximate 2% of construction costs.
4. Sales of property
FONBAPO's inventory of developable land will be completely sold by
1992. Cost of sales will average 60% of sales, including modest
amoumts invested each year.
Expense projections
1. Personnel
The projections show personnel costs increasing at a rate equal to the
inflation of the previous year with the exception of 1984, 1985 and
1986. During these years personnel costs increase an additional 20Z to
reflect the costs of new personnel as FONHAPO builds its
organization and staffs its field offices.
2. Repairs grant buildings
The projections show repairs tO the buildings (received from BANOBRAS
when FONHAPO was created) ending in 1987 under the assumption FONHAPO
will be successful in its intensive campaign to dispose of these units.
3. Administrative expenses
The projections show administrative expenses increasing at a rate equal
to the inflation of the current year.



-68-             CHAPTER V ANNEX
Page 8
4. BANOBRAS commissions
The commissions BANOBRAS charges for processing collections from the
low-cost housing portfolio will end in 1984 according to FONHAPO's
financial management.
5. Depreciation and amortization
Depreciable assets, an immaterial item in FONHAPO's financial
statements, v.111 be completely depreciated by 1992. Because new
equipment requirements are not expected to be significant In relation
to program costs, the projections mke no provision for equipment
acquisitions after 1984.
6.  Old portfolio losses
FONHAPO's uanagement should not be held responsible for losses incurred
in the collection of the low-income mortgage portfolio received when
FONHAPO was created. Several buildings needed extensive repairs and
the computer tab runs of clients were unreliable.
The losses recorded to 1986 in the disposition of the low-cost housing
portfolio reflect the differences between the tab runs and the
accounting records and the up to 20Z discount FONHAPO offers an owner
to settle immediately his outstanding balance. These losses are not
material to the scale of projected operations.

MEXICO
LOW INCOME HOUSING PROJECT
A. FINDINGS OF THE 1984 FONHAPO EVALUATION SAMPLE (FES)l/
Distributional Impact of the FONHAPO Program
1.        FONHAPO's projects are intended for low-income families who,
given their low incomes and independent employment relations, do not
qualify for credits from other public housing programs. Nonetheless, it is
realized that even the mDst affordable of FONHAPO's solutions, through
Sites and Services projects, are not accessible to the very poorest sectors
of the population2/. Table 6.1, presents the best possible match-up of
the available country-wide income distribution data with the information in
the FONHAPO Evaluation Sample (FES) of 1984.
2.        While 40% of the 1980 Census respondents reported incoues below
0.9 of the minimum wage (MW), the equivalent share for the FES is 24%. On
the other hand, 52% of the workers in the FES earned between 0.9 and 1.6
HWX versus 32% in the Census distribution. Admittedly, the comparisOn iS
fraught with problems, beginning with the mis-match of dates and the
difficulty of comparing data on head-of-household income (in the FES) with
the distribution of income between all members of the economically active
population (Census data)3/. The direction of the finding is clear enough,
nevertheless. The FONHATb program, though accessible to the large group of
workers in the lowest Income bracket, is primarily addressed to workers
with earnings close to, but not below the minimum wage.
1/ The survey upon which this analysis is based was conducted between Hay
and July 1984 by FONHAPO's Monitoring and Evaluation Unit. Special
thanks are due to Dr. Noemi Stolovsky and Mr. Ernesto Sodi for access to
this material and for the help given to Mr. Paulo Vieira da Cunha
(Consultant) in the interpretation of the six case studies explored in
this Annex.
21 A recent survey of self-help housing experiences in Mexico City
concludes: "In the past twenty five years squatter settlements... have
not usually hosted the poorest of the urban poor within them' (P.M.Ward:
'The practice and potential of self-help k.ousing in Mexico City', p. 182
in Self-Help Housing:  A Critique, London: Mansell Pub. Co. (1982).  In
a similar vein, a study by the World Bank found that Sites and Services
solutions are generally too expensive for the bottom 20% of families and
serve the upper segment of the groups that can afford them". (IBRD:
Sites and Services and Upgrading, A Review of Bank Assisted Projects,
p. 22, Washington D.C. (1978)).
3/ Note that: a) almost 17% of the EAP failed to disclose income data on
the Census; b) the real value of the KW was higher in 1980 than in 1984;
and c) the income of heads of households is usual'ly higher than that of
other members of the EAP.

3.         In this respect it is important to observe that the main thrust
of the Programa Financiero de Is Vivienda - the competing housing finance
program - is, according to the Census data, directed to the upper 10%
income distribution: a share which, in Mexico is reached with the scant
figure of three or more minimum wages. This group is not represented in
the FONHAPO's program. Note also that the distributional criteria of
FONHAPO's Sites and Services sub-program compares most favorably with that
implied by the output of INFONAVIT, the main public agent in the housing
market (Table 6.2 below).

4.     As shown in Table 6.2, despite the increased progressivity in the
allocation of credits by INFONAVIT (mainly the result of the enlarged
production of Sites and Services in 1983), 15% of the 1983 total was
directed to workers earning more than 2 MW. FONHAPO's equivalent share.
also shown in Table 6.2, is 9Z (in 1984). Moreover, and more importantly,
37% of FONHAPO's credit recipients earned less than the statutory minimum.
The INFONAVIT program does not reach this population.



-102-                   CHAPTER VI ANNEX
Page -.
5.        The foregoing discussion has been based on ii,dividual incomes.  A
preferred measure of either the affordability, or the distributional merit
of the progran would be the level of family income (including transfers and
income-in-kind). Indeed, the distribution (interpersonal welfare)
yardstick should ideally be marked in levels of total faaily income per
capita. Unfortunately, this kind of data is not reported in the published
Census material; and it is simply unknown in the case of INFONAVIT housing
recipients. The FES does report it (though curlously FONHAPO's own
allocational criteria also exclude measures of family income). According
to FES daLa (see Table 6.3), 75% of the families in the Sites and Services
program had monthly earnings below 2 MW. The lower quartile of the
distribution showed levels of income below or up the relevant regional
minimum. This is most certainly a situation of acute poverty, in this case
concentrated in one of the four projects analyzed in the FES.

6.        Variations in sample size and the very small size of some samples
limit reliability of the project specific findings reported in Table 6.3.
With this caveat in mind, the evidence shows that at least some of
FONHAPO's Sites and Services projects are tilted towards the upper range of
the low earning population. For example, of the buyers in project 91
(located in Quintana Roo, within a petroleum exploration zone). 74% had
family earning in excess of 1.7 MW and none had a level below the minimum.
Meanwhile, the fragile evidence of 11 observations for project 139 (in a
depressed urban area of the State of Aguascalientes) would indicate that
nearly the whole project is dir2cted to families with incomes below the
minimum.



-103-                  CHAPTER VI ANNEX
Page 5
7.        Table 6.4 summarizes the distributional characteristics of the
FES and its disaggregation at project level. The mean income of the
household head is 1.33 MW, well below the maximum level established for
FONHAPO (2.5 MW); the average family income is, expectedly, higher but
still below 2 MW. This figure is usually interpreted as the poverty level
for the family. The average per capita income is 0.43 MW, a surprisingly
low figure (approximately US$61 per month). In conformance to the goals of
the program, a substantial share of recipients are workers in the
informal' sector; that is, they are not employees. In fact, given the
earnings data, it is apparent that most employees are themselves workers in
enterprises which operate at the margin of the minimum wage, and perhaps
other legislation. At one extreme (project 139), 73% of the heads of
household are own-account workers with average earnings of 0.75 MW per
month, leading to an extremely low family income per capita of 0.15 MW (16
US$ per month). At the other extreme (project 152), the per capita family
income is nearly four times as lTrge.

8.        Other information gathered by the staff of FONHAPO indicates that
the majority, of workers in the sample could be classified as "semi-skilled"
or better, and many of these had factory employment. Most had worked
continuously over the previous twelve months, and while the data summarized
in paragraph 7 suggest that they were not well paid, there is indication
that they formed a moderately "privileged" and thus financially viable
group in terms of borrowing/repayment capacity.
9.        The data on Table 6.5, also suggest this conclusion.  The
majority of families lived before the project in units with access to
public services (water, light, and to a lesser extent drainage), built of
durable materials, and with access to Loth kitchen and bath. The most
notable deficiencies are the lack of internal bath (51% of the units used
before did not have them) and public drainage (32% of the unit without
access); and except for the latter case, it would not seem that the
"richer' households in the sample enjoyed a disproportionate share of the
desirable characteristics. In comparison to the national average as
measured by the 1980 Census, this particular housing stock is of higher
quality, though it is inferior to the average for Mexico City and other
major metropolitan areas.

10.       In sum, based on an evaluation of FONHAPO's own past experience,
it is evident,that the Sites and Services program is reaching its intended
target population.   It is meeting the demands of the low-income housing
market but not the poorest segment in this market. Within this spectrum,
the program allows for considerable divergence in income most noticeable
across projects;-and, understandably so, for FONHAPO projects are organized
around pre-existing groups or organizations with pre-established levels of
housing demand. The groups are not homogeneous, however. Therefore, there



-105-                CHAPTER VI ANNEX
Page 7
are significant variations in total earnings, household size and levels of
welfare at the project level. Undoubtedly, these deficiencies, if stable,
will be reflected in the uneven development of the sites4/.
The Household budget: Is FONHAPO's solution affordable?
II.       It is commonly assumed that a low-income family could spend UP to
about 20Z of its monthly income on housing. Less than 10% of FONHAPO's
beneficiaries were spending more than that share before the project, and as
it is shown on Table 6.6, practically none of them were spending (or were
planning to spend) in excess of this guideline after the project, though it
is important to note that the definition of housing expenditure is, in this
case, limited to the repayment of the loan and does not include building
and maintenance costs.
Table 6.6: Shares of Expenditures in Food and Housing in Total
Family Income - FONHAPO Evaluation Sample*

12.       Before the project, 22% of the recipients declared no monetary
expenditures for housing. For these families, the new credit will further
constrain the household budget.  Moreover, for the entire sample, the
average share of the budget to be spent on loan repayments alone is
approximately equal to the share of total housing expenditures before the
project. This means that total expenditures on housing (including the
piecemeal production of the new unit) will increase for the average
family. Yet, as we have mentioned, for the FES the average level of
expenditures on housing is typically low with nearly 60% of respondents
declaring an after-project share not exceeding 9% of the household 'budget.
Thus, it is safe to conclude that the mean household could spend an
additional 10% of its budget on housing construction (this would amount to
4/ The data in the FES do not permit a more detailed examination of the
life-cycle of the families in the program. Some indication point in the
direction of typically young fansllies who have not reached the toP of
their earning potential.



-106-               CHAPTER VI ANNEX
Page 8
approximately 2 MWs per year, on average, currently the cost of
construction is 27.6 MW per m2). The lingering doubt in an examination of
the ability (vs. willingness) to spend on housing, concerns the weight of
the other itpms in the budget, most importantly, food (Table 6.6).
13.       Returning to Table 6.6, we observe that nearly 30% of the
families spent only 40-55% of their budget on food and that no more than a
quarter of them, spent in excess of 55%. When combined with housing, the
two items account for more than 60% of the budget of 32% of the families
before the project, and 40% of the families after the project. Thus, it
appears that most families should be able to meet the additional demand for
house building funds. Indeed, the proportion of families with severe
budget limitations (i.e. spending more than 3/4 of their income on food and
housing alone) is relatively low, below 15%.
14.       To conclude, FONHAPO's Sites and Services program is affordable
to its target population even when proper account is taken of the required
complement of funds for progressive house building. There are however
indications that a minority of credit recipients (perhaps as many as 15%)
will face difficulties to meet the additional costs of construction. For
this reason (and considering that in the consolidation of a Sites and
Services project those factors, which relate to income and the creation of
an investment surplus at the household level, should be the most important
in explaining the levels of improvement that can be achieved) it is
advisable that supplementary loans be extended to the original credit
recipients, for the purpose of house building. The FONHAPO program allows
and presently encourages such loans.
Housing Conditions After the Credit
15.       The FES contains data on 20 households who had already built a
housing unit on the new, improved lot (approximately 30% of the sample).
Most of those who built are among the poorest in the sample: 45% of the
families who had built new units belonged to the lowest quartile of
income. The units are built in two projects, one finished in June 1984
(Rincon del Ramos, Aguascalientes, with 664 lots), and another still in
construction at the time of the survey (Chatumel, Quintana Roo with 430
lots). It appears that in neither case there has been as yet a substantial
gain in living standards, though admittedly the projects are at the very
early stage of consolidation. To a large extent this is due to the
maintenance of previous housing standards: mostly one-room units but with
internal kitchen and bath. Both projects are to be served by public light
and water, but only one of them will have public drainage facilities.
Though deficient in some respect, this pattern of supply maintains the
pre-project level of access to public utilities by the households. In the
decision to approve loans for Sites and Services projects, FONHAPO takes
into consideration the provision of basic (i.e. off-site) urban services.
This means that, as a norm, Sites and Services projects have standard
access to transport, education and health facilities. However, because
they are generally located in the periphery of urban areas, it is expected
that residents will have longer trips to work, shopping and recreation.



CHAPTER VI ANNEX
-107-                Page 9
B. NOTES ON THE MEASUREMENT OF COSTS AND BENEFITS
1.        In evaluating costs and benefits, we have focused on the changes
in total consumption (or net value added) which are likely to result from
the proposed project. Costs and benefits have been measured in domestic
market prices. The goods and services involved are for the most part
non-tradeable. When tradeable, as in the case of steel, the quantities
consldered would not affect significantly the foreign balance. Therefore,
the introduction of either border pricing or of appropriate conversion
factors would unnecessarily complicate an already complex valuation
problem. Note further that no deduction has been made for the Value Added
Tax (IVA), which normally is charged On all final sales. By law, purchases
for the production of Social Interest Housing (VIS) are exempt from this
tax, which does not appear in FONHAPO's own accounting costs. Shadow
prices, applicable only to unskilled labor which constitutes a minor
fraction of total construction costs, were judged not to have a major
impact on the rates of return and were not considered.
2 .       For the calculation of the ERRs it has been assuxed that the new
stock lasts for 25 years from the date the project begun. A yearly
allowance of 1Z of total construction costs has been made for maintenance,
with uniform incidence for the first 10 years following the completion of
the unit. The residual value of stock and land at the end of the project
cycle has been ignored. Since all housing is progressively built, the
following conventions were used in the analysis of the typical unit in the
standard project: (a) the unit was defined as a 3-room construction (each
room with 12 m2) progressively built in the case of Sites and Services
projects, or delivered 'finished' in the case of start-up dwellings (with a
total construction area of 40.50 m2); (b) data on costs provided by FONHAPO
were based on the first semester 1984 averages. Accordingly, the cost per
m2 of construction was established at Mex$16,371; the market price of a
standard lot with 11.80 m2 at Mex$56,813 and the cost of urbanization at
Mex$l,278 per m2; (c) since no account could be taken of differences in
construction costs and/or in layouts favored by own account builders, both
of. which would result in additional benefits for the units, we have assumed
equal imputed rent for both the -finished' start-up units (with 40.50 m2)
and the 'finished" self-constructed unit (with 36.00m2); and (d) a cash
flow sequence defined as shown in the following Table 6.7.
3.        Given the difficulties In imputing a market price to a product
that does not yet exist and is not easily compared to others, the
measurement of future streams of rental values is fraught with problems.
And, in this case, the task is further complicated for the project does not
involve the production of standardized, ready-to-market units. Rather, the
units are to be built progressively and the price of half a bouse is
probably not equal to one-half the price of a finished house. Moreover, it
is one of the merits of this project, that houses are to be produced in
accordance to the tastes, willingness and capabilities of the families
involved. Thus, in this case, the estimate of Imputed rental Values
requires additional assumptions to current practice.

4.     More than one half of the household covered by the FONHAPO
Evaluation Sample (Annex II B) were previously renters (see Table 6.8 below
and Table 8 in Chapter VI). Of these, approximately one half used to rent
finished units and only one third, finished units including bath and
kitchen. Curiously, renters of the nore endowed units used to pay lower
rents per room - a result that may reflect the increasing instability of
the samples (due to their small sizes) and/or the possibility that
"finished units" are in fact dilapidated and rent-controlled portions of
the stock. Note that according to the FONHAPO Evaluation Sample, average
income decline as quality supposedly "improves" when, in fact, one would
expect the opposite to occur.
ERR Sensitivity Analysis
5.        Concerning the economic benefits of the project, in addition to
the preferred set of assumptions (termed A) in Table 8 on page 36, two
other sets were considered: B) a sensitivity experiment supposing a 25%
reduction in the imputed rental value implied by the assumptions in set A);
and C) the value which results from combining the use of the average income
reported in the first row of Table 6.2 with a one standard deviation
increases in the share of the head's income to be spent for rent. Set C)
depicts the following simulation: on the one hand, a greater willingness
of households to spend income on housing because they are purchasing
(building) a home of their own (which leads to an increase in the share of
housing expenditures in the household budget); on the other hand, set C)
supposes that households are incapable of raising their income levels over
time (which leads to their building a unit without market improvement in
quality with respect to their previous situation(*).
6.        Concerning the economic costs of the project, two alternatives
are explored in Table 8. For Sites and Services projects, under
alternative a) the share of adm-iinistration costs (and the implicit profit
margin of commercial builders) in total project costs are deducted from the
construction investments. According to FONHAPO's practice, this share is
usually set at 30% of total investment costs. Alternative b) corresponds
to full cost pricing on the assumption that self-built practices are
uncommon and do not amount to a large share of total construction costs.
For Progressive Construction projects, alternative a) assumes a one-year
production cycle which in fact over-estimates current FONHAPO experience.
Alternative b), in this case, presumes a conservatively long, two-year
project cycle.
() The income reported in Table 7 (page 35) is that of the head of
household only. Considering total family income, the average FES
household spends a below-average share of the budget for housing. Thus
the assumption of an increased share could be realistically met even
with stable incomes.

TERMS OF REFERENCE: MEXICO HOUSING FINANCE SECTOR STUDY
Issues in the Housing Finance System of Mexico
1.        The three main questions facing the housing finance system of
Mexico at present are:
a) How well is the system adjusting to the current period of high
inflation and reduced economic activity?
The current housing finance system of Mexico has been developed
incrementally in an environment of considerably lower inflation than
at present and the resource base of the sector has been seriously
eroded by inflation since 1979, dramatically so since 1982. In early
1984, the Government has taken three major decisions: it has
reorganized the public institutional framework, it has increased the
allocation of resources to housing, and it has changed the onlending
conditions of the publicly sponsored agencies in the housing market.
The adjustment of lending conditions to inflation has been only
partial and it has not covered all major housing finance
institutions. There are therefore two unanswered questions regarding
the current transition: first, will the variable rate mortgages
recently designed lead to a more complete adjustment and full capital
recovery as inflation declines; second, how quickly and with what
kinds of mortgage instruments is it possible to improve the various
payroll funds such as INFONAVIT which have yet to make the necessary
operational adjustments to their new economic environment.
b) What are the possible avenues for increasing the coordination of the
variety of financial institutions intervening in the housing sector?
The Bank's public housing sector report (*) shows the complexity of
the housing finance system of Mexico and its fragmented nature.
Simplifying somewhat, the Mexican housing finance system is based on
three distincts approaches which have led to the emergence of three
differential sub-systems most clearly distinguishable by the sources
of their funds: (a) the banking sector; (b) the special housing
funds financed by payroll taxes; and (c) the public housing programs
financed through Government equity and budgetary allocations.
Flow of funds information on the total housing finance system that is
comprehensive and up to date is not readily available. During the
period 1977-1981 the total amount of annual housing financing
available was of the order of US$1.8 billion. The composition of
(*) Public Housing Policy in Mexico, Urban Sector Report No. 4993-ME (Green
Cover) IBRD, June 1984.



-112-         ~~CHAPTER VIII ANN~EX
-112-              Page 2
total housing finance resources was approximately 24% from the
banking sector, 63% from the housing funds and about 13% from
Government programs. The various social housing programs which cut
across the three sources of funds accounted for little over one half
of total sector resources. In 1984, the housing sector received a
high level of priority in order to stimulate the economy and to
soften the impact of the economic crisis on middle and low income
households. The total amount of resources earmarked for housing was
US$2.7 billion with a planned composition of financing as follows:
45% banking sector, 40% housing funds 15% public programs.
c)  What kind of actions are possible to improve the availability of
housing finance to an increasing proportion of the Mexican
population?
All the evidence points to a rapidly growing demand for housing
fueled by population growth and urbanization over the next two
decades, and housing has been defined as a priority sector in
Mexico. On the other hand, the need to restore the strength of the
economy dictates that national priority be given to raising the level
of domestic savings and to increasing the efficiency of the existing
productive system. In the case of housing, such national priorities
imply that attention be given first to capital recovery and the
ability of financial institutions to expand their program in a
sustained and viable way without requiring external injections of
scarce financial resources. Increasing the efficiency of the housing
finance system also means serving a wider proportion of the Mexican
households. Therefore a review and technical analysis of the current
situation is necessary on three fronts:  resource mobilization for
housing finance; lending policies and lending instruments; an
evaluation of the structure of the present system and of coordination
requirements, including smn examination of the support facilities
already in place or needed such as deposit insurance, mortgage
insurance, refinancing facilities.
2.        It should be noted that this study's priority is on the
improvemtnt of the financial institutions serving housing but that the
Mexican informal sector is very large. The growth of the informal sector
is due for a significant part to non-financial constraints which should be
the object of another study. The main area of overlap lies in improving
access to financing for low and middle-income households through improved
lending policies.
Objectives of the Study
3.        The objectives of the study are:
a) to describe in a comprehensive manner the current structure of the
Mexican housing finance system and the various institutions which
make up that system;



CHAPTER VIII ANNEX
-113-                Page 3
b) to inventory and analyze the financial instruments that are presently
available to finance the various phases of the development process;
c) to provide an evaluation of the performance of the various
institutional components of the housing finance system: efficiency
in resource mDbilization, capacity to adjust to the current
inflationary environment, ability to finance various household income
groups;
d) to propose possible strategies for the evolution of the housing
finance system (instruments, institutions) which could improve its
contribution to national savings, increase the coordination and
efficiency of rhe system in an inflationary environment and make
financing available to a higher proportion of Mexican households,
particularly middle and low income households. These strategic
proposals would cover both financial instruments and institutional
interactions.
An Illustrative Outline
4.        The outline which follows is not meant to represent the final
structure of the report, rather it is intended to be a vehicle for the
determination of the scope of study, for the clearer identification of the
technical work to be performed and as a means to determine the allocation
of tasks among participants.
Part I. CONTEXT
5.        This section should draw as much as possible from existing
sources. It should cover:
(i) The Housing Sector
a) income and housing in the 1980s: an overview of the sources of income
growth in the past decade, a close analysis of these sources and a
forecast of income growth;
b)  price of housing and tenure choice in the 1980s: demographic trends,
household formation and impact on housing prices. Balance between
formal and informal housing;
c) housing production in the 1980s: distinction between residential and
non-residential construction; analysis of the statistical record by
type of units and by local housing markets; projection of the
composition of output in the 1980s.
(ii) The Housing Finance Institutions and Windows:
a) an overview of the banking sector in Mexico and latest trends;
b) the Banking sector, FOVI-FOGA;
c) the housing funds: complete inventory description of major types;
d) public programs (such as FONHAPO).



-114-                  CHAPTER VIII ANNEX
Page 4
Part II. EVALUATION
6.        Housing Finance and Housing Demand:  The Broider Issues
a) financing housing: is the cost of financing housing out of line with
costs of financing other investments or activities?
b) inflation, nominal interest rates and housing demand: cash flow and
affordability problems created by high levels of inflation and
fluctuating interest rates.
7.        Current Financial Instruments:  Description and evaluation.  The
objective of this section would be to establish the extent to whicbs
identical borrowers may be fs:ing widely different financing according to
sources and the degree of consistent pricing fir essentially identical
household characteristics and/or housing units. Given tht: fragmented
institutional structure of Mexico, another question to be addressed in this
section is how households who have been successful in financing their house
might be combining loans from different sources and what the impact of such
multiple borrowing might be on their income cash flow and credit risk.
It would cover:
a) types of financing for various real estate development activities
(land acquisition, construction, long term finance);
b) variations in financial terms offered by various institutions.
Performance of the Various Institutions
8.        In spite of the magnitude of the task, it would Se very important
for the study to have maximum coverage of all the institutions and agencies
financing housing. Since the comparison of financial terms offered to
borrowers will have been performed in the earlier section the focus of this
section would be on three major issues:
a) sources of funds and mobilization of Fe-ings;
b) volume, types of loans and borrowers;
c) capital recovery and financie: viability.
9.        The performance of the major individual institutions within each
of the three components of the present housing fi.nance system (banking
sector, housing funds, and government-firanced programs) will be evaluated.
Part III. RECOMMENDATIONS
10.       As noted earlier, the three major issue. facing the Mexican
Housing Finance system at present are: its degree of adaptation to high
levels of inflation, effective coordination mechanisms and reduced
fragmentation of the institutional structure; improved access to financing
on the part of households. Recommendation regarding the system will cover
two major parts: improvement of the financial instruments and
institutional improvement.



-115-                     CHAPTER VIII ANNEX
Page 5
11.       Improvements of financial instruments:  this section should
discuss several important facts of the "mortgage menu problem" in Mexico,
i.e. the needs of the borrowers (inflation and the "tilt problem"), the
needs of the lenders (maturity matching, profitability and capacity to
expand) and the needs of the long-term investors (capital recovery,
inflation and interest levels, credit risk, call protection, etc.).
a) policies toward adjustments to inflation (adjusting without
comprehensive indexing);
b) interest rates;
c) types of financing needed for various components of the housing
sector (land, development, construction finance, mortgage finance).
12.       Institutional improvements and  financial viability:  more uoney
less important than its better use, especially in the housing funds;
systemic issues of institutional coordinations in Mexico:
-   money and capital market constraints (competing claims on financial
savings);
-   improvement of institutional set-up missing institutional mechanims?
-   non-financial constraints (land, legalizatien, standards and
controls) for the record only.
13.       Strategies for housing finance:
a) Major issues
b) Strengths and limitations of the present structure
c) Possible directions.
